The Appeals were discussed for four years. They were ultimately "deferred for further consideration." In Silverborn administrative language, this meant the same thing it means in every bureaucracy in history: no.

---

#### From Reform to Ruin

The failure of the Appeals broke Mettavuur's faith.

900 years believing the system could be reformed from within. The Appeals were the culmination. When the system rejected them — politely, patiently, with all the condescending respect a thousand-year-old institution could muster — Mettavuur arrived at a conclusion that would define the next three thousand years:

**The system cannot be reformed. It must be replaced. The Silverborn will not give up power voluntarily. Power must be taken from them.**

This conclusion was not wrong. The Accord WAS rigid. The Silverborn WERE resistant to structural change. The mortal grievances WERE legitimate. Every argument in the Appeals was factually correct.

What followed was not a response proportional to the problem. The gap between "this system needs to change" and "everyone in this system needs to die" is the gap where Mettavuur became a monster. The precise moment of that transition is not recorded.

What is recorded: Mettavuur stopped attending Council sessions around Year 1,530 and began traveling.

---

#### The Recruitment

Mettavuur spent roughly 170 years building the coalition. They did it the way a Bridge-Speaker would — through conversation, through listening, through understanding exactly what each group needed to hear.

**To the Humans:** "You are the majority. You build everything. You receive nothing. Under a new order, your numbers will mean what they should: power."

**To the Thalessi:** "You are the natural successors. When the Accord falls, you will inherit what was always meant to be yours."

**To the Gorundai:** "Your traditions are not barbaric. Under a new order, no outside force will tell you when you can fight."

**To the Khari:** "Your paths are stolen knowledge. Under a new order, the paths belong to you."

**To the Rathai:** "Your name on your work. That's all."

**To the Varrek:** "Access. Full, unrestricted access to study what you choose."

**To the Oni:** "No more monitors. No more schedules. Live at full volume."

Every promise was tailored. Every grievance was real. Mettavuur did not invent the resentment — they focused it.

And to each group, Mettavuur told a different story about the endgame — the Humans heard "relocation," the Thalessi heard "retirement," the Gorundai heard "combat defeat," the Khari heard "departure," the Rathai heard "integration," the Varrek heard "study," the Oni heard "who cares."

None of these was the plan. The plan was genocide. But nobody knew that except Mettavuur.

---

#### The Coalition's Structure

By Year 1,680, Mettavuur had assembled a coalition spanning every mortal race on both continents:

- **Haessig Cadre** (Humans): Political descendants of Cormund Haessig, organized as a secret society within Ossuvael's administrative class. Led by **Orren Haessig**, Cormund's great-great-granddaughter.
- **The Vaeluur Circle** (Thalessi): Scholars and diplomats in Aethrennis's tradition. Led by **Thalruun Essovane**, the finest Thalessi strategist of the Second Age.
- **Kelvosh Confederacy** (Gorundai): Druuga Kelvosh's alliance, now expanded to twelve clans.
- **Renn Network** (Khari): Tashka Renn's unmarked paths served as the coalition's communication infrastructure.
- **Dossal Guilds** (Rathai): Engineering guilds that controlled the infrastructure of every major city — the coalition's sabotage arm.
- **Gallavan Societies** (Varrek): Brenoch Gallavan's intellectual network provided the ideological framework.
- **Setsuda Warriors** (Oni): Festival-networks masking military organization. Seasonal gatherings included combat training. The Bridge-Speakers saw celebration. The violence training underneath it was also real.

---

#### What Mettavuur Wanted

The coalition members each wanted something specific. Mettavuur wanted something none of them understood.

Mettavuur wanted to be necessary.

The genocide was not the goal. The genocide was the method. The goal was a world that could not function without Mettavuur — a world where the only person who understood the full picture, who held all the threads, who knew every secret, was the one who had orchestrated the collapse.

This is what separates Mettavuur from a mere tyrant. A tyrant seizes power. Mettavuur designed a system in which their power was structurally invisible and therefore permanent. The Masqarat would govern. The Pillars would enforce. The Wardensea would patrol. And above all of it, unseen, unaccountable, and irreplaceable, Mettavuur would remain — the only living being who knew why the world was the way it was.

The cover-up was not an afterthought. It was the point.

---

### Part 3: The Betrayal and Genocide (~Years 1,700–1,803)

#### Year 1,702 — The Night of Falling Canopies

The attack began at the Eddan Trees.

Mettavuur understood that the Silverborn's power flowed from the groves. The Trees were anchors — each grove sustained the Silverborn community around it, provided God Fruits for distribution, and served as a physical connection to the divine energy that made the Silverborn more than mortal. Destroying the Trees would sever them from the source of their power and begin the biological degradation that would reduce them from demigods to diminished Lunarians.

Eight Trees. Eight simultaneous strikes. One night.

**The attack plan was Thalruun Essovane's masterwork.** The Thalessi strategist designed the logistics believing he was planning a controlled seizure — occupation of the groves, displacement of the guardians, transfer of Tree-management to a mortal body. What Essovane did not know was that Mettavuur had given separate orders to the Gorundai strike teams: burn everything.

**The First Tree (western Orenthas):** Kelvosh Confederacy warriors, 400 strong, under Rathai-engineered smoke screen. The grove guardians overwhelmed within an hour. The Gorundai did not take prisoners. The Tree took three days to fully burn. The pillar of silver-white smoke was visible from a hundred miles.

**The Second Tree (eastern Orenthas, Ossuvael):** The most complex strike. The Haessig Cadre disabled the city's internal security (they were the bureaucrats who ran those systems). Dossal's teams sealed the underground passages. The Setsuda Warriors hit the grove like a festival gone murderous. Seventeen Silverborn died defending the Second Tree. The Tree burned.

**The Third Tree (central Caldruun):** Kessanet's grove — the strongest guardian force of any Eddan site. Forty-one Silverborn defenders. The coalition committed over 2,000 fighters, including three God Fruit users armed through the Khari black-market Fruit trade. The battle lasted six days. Every guardian died. The Tree burned on the seventh.

**Trees Four through Eight** fell in similar fashion over eleven days. By the end of the second week, all eight Eddan Trees on the settled continents were ash.

**The Ninth Tree** was not attacked. It existed on the Sundren — a continent no confirmed mortal expedition had reached. Mettavuur knew about it and said nothing. The ninth Tree still grows on the Sundren, tended by nothing but Bhoraam's unchecked ecology, producing God Fruits that no organized system distributes.

---

#### The Hunting — Years 1,702 to 1,780

The Trees burned in weeks. The Silverborn died over decades.

Once the groves fell, the 180,000 surviving Silverborn scattered. Without the Trees, their connection to divine energy began to fade — flight became harder, the glow dimmed, Vision grew unreliable. They were still formidable — a Silverborn warrior could fight at Forged tier or above even without Tree-support — but they were diminishing. And they were outnumbered 10,000 to 1.

Mettavuur organized the hunting with Bridge-Speaker precision:

**To the Humans:** "Report their locations."
**To the Thalessi:** "Capture and confinement is the humane option."
**To the Gorundai:** "Hunt."
**To the Khari:** "Track."
**To the Rathai:** "Seal their hiding places."
**To the Varrek:** "Study what remains."
**To the Oni:** "Finish it."

---

#### The Resistance

Not every Silverborn ran. Not every mortal complied.

**Aurvath's Last Stand (Year 1,704):** The First Speaker organized a defense of roughly 8,000 Silverborn on Orenthas's northern coast. Aurvath offered full transfer of governmental authority. Full surrender of the petition system. Full Silverborn withdrawal from political life. The only condition: stop killing us. Mettavuur's answer, delivered through a visibly shaken Thalruun Essovane: "The transition requires a clean break. History cannot have two versions. The Silverborn must become a memory."

Aurvath dismissed the 8,000 into small groups — families, bonded pairs, individuals — and told them to scatter. Hide. Survive. Remember. Aurvath himself stood on the northern coast with forty-two volunteers and held it for six days against 12,000 coalition fighters. His King could immobilize a battlefield. But King cannot stop arrows from beyond visual range. And it cannot stop exhaustion. Aurvath died on the sixth day. His body was burned. His name was erased from every Accord record. Lunarian oral tradition preserved it.

**Ruvaan's Choice (Year 1,709):** Ruvaan the Grieving gathered children and non-combatants, loaded them onto three ships, and sailed into the Howling Gyre. The coalition ships turned back at the Gyre's outer edge. What happened to Ruvaan's group inside the Gyre is unknown. The iron-hulled stations dotting the Gyre's inner ring — the ones with charts in a script no modern linguist can read — may be Ruvaan's work.

**Solmund's Gambit (Year 1,715):** The Last Singer was specifically targeted by Mettavuur. The sovereign understood that Solmund carried the complete oral record. Mettavuur sent a dedicated hunting team — seven God Fruit users and forty Dominion-trained soldiers. They tracked Solmund across Caldruun for three years. Solmund used those three years to split their memory — dividing the complete archive across seven Silverborn descendants, each receiving a fragment of the whole. No single descendant carried enough to reconstruct the full history, but together, the seven fragments formed a complete record. Solmund was caught in Year 1,718. Killed. Modern Lunarian memory-keepers believe they carry Solmund's fragments. The Masqarat believes this is myth. Hettashi knows it is not.

**Mortal Resisters:**

**Thalruun Essovane's Defection (Year 1,706):** The strategist realized within days that the operation had exceeded its mandate. He had planned a transfer of power. What happened was a massacre. He attempted to contact surviving Silverborn council members to negotiate ceasefire. Mettavuur intercepted the communication. Essovane was found dead three days later. Official story: Silverborn retaliation.

**The Gharmund Refusal (Year 1,710):** Three Gorundai clans — the **Morrhgal**, the **Ashettar**, and the **Vunndek** — refused to participate. Their reasoning was pragmatic: the Silverborn had maintained the hot springs, treated injuries, and mediated disputes. Killing them was bad strategy. The three clans declared neutrality and offered sanctuary to fleeing Silverborn. The coalition besieged the Morrhgal stronghold for forty days. Over 2,000 died on all sides. Modern Gorundai who trace lineage to these three clans carry it as a quiet distinction.

**Khari Path-Hiders (Year 1,712 onward):** At least four independent warrens — the Shallowfoot, the Nightrunners, the Keelbreak, and the Thornwarren — deliberately falsified tracking reports to lead coalition hunters away from Silverborn refugees. This saved an estimated 3,000-4,000 lives. Tashka Renn discovered the falsification and expelled them from the Network. The warrens accepted expulsion and continued hiding Silverborn. This split persists in modern Khari warren politics.

---

#### Varruhn — The Second Moon's End

Varruhn was not consumed during the First Age. It was consumed during the Second Age — specifically, during the burning of the Eddan Trees.

The Trees drew sustenance from Varruhn's light. When the coalition burned the eight Trees, the divine energy they contained discharged upward — a cascade of released power. The Trees had been absorbing Varruhn's light for over a thousand years. When they burned, they returned that energy catastrophically, all at once. Varruhn absorbed the feedback. Its golden surface cracked. Over seven months (Year 1,702 to 1,703), the moon destabilized, fragmented, and was consumed — not by any actor but by the thermodynamic consequences of destroying eight divine artifacts simultaneously.

The night Varruhn died, every Silverborn on the planet felt it. The last thread connecting them to their divine heritage snapped. Kessuura — the god of storms — screamed. Not metaphorically. Sailors across both oceans reported a sound at the edge of hearing that lasted for three days.

---

#### The Death Toll

By Year 1,780, the hunting was functionally complete.

| Category | Number |
|---|---|
| Silverborn alive at Year 1,702 | ~180,000 |
| Killed in grove attacks (Year 1,702) | ~2,400 |
| Killed in Aurvath's last stand | ~43 volunteers + unknown coalition casualties |
| Killed during the hunting (1,702-1,780) | ~155,000+ |
| Disappeared into the Howling Gyre (Ruvaan's group) | ~2,500 (fate unknown) |
| Confirmed survivors by Year 1,780 | ~20,000 |

The survivors were scattered, diminished, and terrified. Their wings were failing. Their lifespans, which had been indefinite, began to cap. Their silver eyes dulled. Their glow faded. The process took generations, but it had already begun.

The Silverborn became the Lunarians. Not through any single act of punishment, but through the slow, grinding consequence of having the source of their power destroyed. The genocide didn't just kill 160,000 beings. It killed the species. What survived was something lesser — something that remembered being more.

---

#### The Ennead's Response

The gods watched.

**Hettashi** experienced the genocide as a sustained assault on their domain. Every Silverborn who died took memories with them. Hettashi began the subtle protective work around the Lunarian lineages that carry Solmund's archive. This vigil has lasted three thousand years.

**Kessuura** raged. The Howling Gyre intensified dramatically — storms that should have been seasonal became permanent, and the Vorrenmaw's surrounding waters became functionally impassable. The Vorrenmaw was sealed behind a wall of storms.

**Fennaeth** felt every broken bond. The Silverborn-mortal relationships severed across the world simultaneously. Fennaeth reported the sensation was "like hearing a symphony collapse into noise, instrument by instrument, until only the drums remained." The drums were the surviving bonds between individuals — Khari path-hiders protecting refugees, the Morrhgal offering sanctuary. Fennaeth sustained these. It was all they could do.

**Olvassyr** was silent. The god of civilization watched an entire civilization be destroyed and said nothing. This silence has been interpreted as either paralysis or approval. Olvassyr has never clarified. The other Ennead members have never forgiven the ambiguity.

**Durrakha** counted. Every death. Every one. "I did not approve. I did not prevent. I received."

**Vasuur, Bhoraam, and Meyassin** withdrew further, reducing engagement with mortal affairs to near-zero. They have not re-engaged since.

**Tavrendis** — sealed in the Vorrenmaw — was beyond reach. But Silverborn oral tradition records: when the Eddan Trees burned, the Vorrenmaw's spin accelerated. For approximately seven hours, the Maelstrom's pull increased by an estimated 40%, dragging three coalition ships past the Clutch. They were never seen again. Something in the Vorrenmaw noticed when the Trees died.

---

### Part 4: The Cover-Up (Years 1,780–1,803)

#### The Proto-Masqarat

With the Silverborn destroyed and the coalition victorious, the world needed a new governing structure. Mettavuur had planned for this.

The coalition's mortal leaders expected to divide power — each race governing its own territory, cooperating as equals in a loose confederation. This was what Mettavuur had promised. It was not what Mettavuur delivered.

**The Ossuvael Compact (Year 1,783):** The coalition leaders convened in the ruins of Ossuvael. Mettavuur attended as an "advisory presence" — a Silverborn survivor offering institutional knowledge. Nobody questioned why a Silverborn was advising the coalition that had just destroyed the Silverborn. Mettavuur had maintained their cover throughout — the sympathizer who had "seen the Silverborn's failures from within." The coalition leaders believed Mettavuur was one of the good ones.

The Compact established:
- A unified governmental structure over both continents
- A military arm to maintain order (the proto-Wardensea)
- A judicial system based on Gorundai arbitration (modified by Thalessi legal theory)
- A trade framework incorporating Kolmari-predecessor merchant networks
- A **historical commission** tasked with "cataloging and preserving the record of the transition"

The historical commission was Mettavuur's masterstroke. Ostensibly preservation, actually destruction. Staffed with loyalists who would follow instructions without questioning.

---

#### The First Erasures (Years 1,785–1,803)

**Stage 1 — Reclassification (1,785-1,790):** All Silverborn-language documents reclassified as "historical security materials." Stated reason: the documents contained "references to divine mechanisms that could be misused." Real reason: the documents described what had just happened.

**Stage 2 — Controlled Destruction (1,790-1,795):** Commission teams "consolidated" Silverborn archives into central repositories for "preservation." Once consolidated, the archives were burned. The fires were classified. The smoke was not.

**Stage 3 — The Ontological Hazard (Year 1,797):** Mettavuur coined the term. The classification was applied to Silverborn script, all Second Age records, Eddan Tree grove ruins, and any pre-Compact archaeological site. The beauty was its unfalsifiability. How do you prove reading a text ISN'T a threat to reality? The burden of proof fell on anyone who wanted to study the material — and the only body authorized to evaluate the proof was Mettavuur's commission. Circular by design.

**Stage 4 — The Erasure Clerks (Year 1,800):** The historical commission reorganized into a permanent security division. Their mandate: identify, contain, and destroy any surviving Second Age records, artifacts, or oral traditions. Their first major operation: the **Ossuvael Burial** — complete demolition of the former capital, the ruins of the Second Tree, the Canopy Hall, and the surviving archives. The site was physically buried under an engineered landslide. The location was removed from all maps. Modern geography has no record of Ossuvael. The city that was the administrative center of the world for 800 years exists in no chart, no text, no Grimoire database. Beneath an unremarkable hillside on Orenthas's eastern coast: the foundations of 300,000 lives, and the stump of a god's tree.

---

#### The Covenant of Silence

The coalition leaders were given a choice: accept the new order and prosper, or refuse and be simplified.

**Orren Haessig** accepted. Became the first Human administrative director of the Compact's Orenthas division. Died at sixty-eight without ever learning that Mettavuur was a Silverborn.

**Druuga Kelvosh** accepted. Received territorial sovereignty for her confederation. Lived to eighty-one, considered a hero. Never asked where the hunting orders had come from.

**Tashka Renn** accepted. Path registration abolished. Unmarked paths became official routes. Renn died at seventy-three. Her descendants still run the primary Khari intelligence network.

**Vekk Dossal** accepted. The "Accord Arch" was renamed the "Dossal Arch." It is still called that. This was the only promise Mettavuur kept.

**Brenoch Gallavan** refused. The Varrek philosopher recognized the historical commission for what it was: a suppression apparatus using his own principle to hoard knowledge. He attempted to publish a complete account of Second Age events. Found dead in Year 1,799. Official record: natural causes. He was fifty-three.

**Rokkhan Setsuda** refused. The Oni festival-chief took her warriors into the Sennaku Highlands and declared independence. The Compact sent a pacification force. Setsuda destroyed it. Sent a second. Setsuda destroyed that too. The Compact stopped sending forces. The Sennaku Highlands have operated semi-independently ever since. Modern Oni highland culture — the one Kuldhara Mabaan defends — is Setsuda's direct legacy.

---

#### Year 1,803 — The End of the Second Age

The Second Age ends with the formal establishment of the proto-Masqarat (originally "The Compact" — the name "Masqarat" came later, adopted during the Third Age).

What the world looked like at the transition:

- Eight Eddan Trees: ash.
- The Silverborn: 20,000 survivors, scattered, diminishing, terrified. Becoming Lunarians.
- The ninth Eddan Tree: growing on the Sundren, unknown, unattended, producing Fruits that fall to the ground and rot or are consumed by wildlife.
- Varruhn: gone. One moon. Harsher weather. The Howling Gyre thickening.
- The Vorrenmaw: spinning faster since the Trees burned. Something noticed.
- God Fruits: cycling. No new Fruits produced (the Trees are gone). Existing Fruits recycle when users die — reappearing at random. The chaotic modern Fruit economy begins here.
- Dominion: spreading naturally. The divine origin forgotten within two generations.
- The Ennead: withdrawn. Nine gods watching and not speaking to each other.
- Mettavuur: invisible. A Silverborn sitting in the shadow above a mortal government, writing directives in a language the government has classified as an ontological hazard. The greatest mass murderer in the world's history, governing the world they murdered their way into. Alone, immortal, and necessary.

The Third Age begins when the first generation of mortals who don't remember the Second Age takes power. By Year 1,860, the Second Age is history. By Year 2,000, it's myth. By Year 3,000, it's a crime to research.

The lie is complete. The Lunarians remember. The Sundren grows. The Vorrenmaw spins. And somewhere in the world, seven descendants carry pieces of a song that could undo three thousand years of silence.

---

## 5. THE THIRD AND FOURTH AGES (Years 1,803–5,540) — THE REMEMBERED HISTORY

### THE THIRD AGE (~Years 1,803–3,400): The Age of Fracture

The Third Age begins in the hangover of the Second. The Compact — the proto-government Mettavuur built from the coalition's ashes — exists, but barely. It has administrative authority over Orenthas and limited influence on Caldruun. It has no military arm worth naming. It has a bureaucratic structure designed by a Silverborn traitor who needs it functional enough to maintain the cover-up but weak enough to never threaten their invisible control.

The result: sixteen hundred years of decentralized chaos.

---

#### The Compact's Slow Drift (Years 1,803–2,800)

The Compact survived because Mettavuur designed it to survive — not to thrive, but to persist. A government strong enough to suppress Second Age research, coordinate the Erasure Clerks, and maintain basic trade infrastructure. Too weak to centralize real power in any mortal hands.

Mettavuur achieved this through structural handicaps built into the Compact's charter:
- **Rotating leadership** among mortal races, ensuring no faction accumulated enough institutional memory to challenge the sovereign's directives
- **Fragmented military authority** — regional garrisons answered to local governors, not a central command
- **No permanent capital** — the administrative seat rotated between three cities on Orenthas
- **The Erasure Clerks as the only permanent institution** — classified, above the law, answering to a chain of command that terminated in Mettavuur

This design worked. For a thousand years, the Compact drifted — functional enough to maintain the lie, dysfunctional enough that no mortal leader could threaten it. The mortal races filled the power vacuum with their own ambitions.

---

#### The Thalessi-Morventhi Split (Years ~2,100–2,400)

The split did not happen in a day. It fermented across three centuries, rooted in Second Age guilt the Thalessi could not resolve.

**Phase 1 — The Remembrance Faction (~Year 2,100):** By the early Third Age, the Second Age was already fading from living memory for most races. But the Thalessi lived 300-400 years. Some alive in Year 2,100 were the grandchildren of figures who had stood in the Ossuvael coalition. Among them, a faction emerged — scholars and moralists who believed the Thalessi owed a debt for what Thalruun Essovane had designed and Aethrennis Vaeluur had allowed through silence. They called themselves the **Vetha-moren** — "Those Who Turn Toward" in early Thalessi. They wanted internal cultural reckoning.

**Phase 2 — The Refusal (~Year 2,200):** The mainstream Thalessi establishment rejected the Vetha-moren. The Compact's Erasure Clerks monitored Second Age discourse. Any institution preserving suppressed knowledge would be targeted. The establishment's position was pragmatic: survival required forgetting. The Vetha-moren were expelled from academic institutions, stripped of civic status, and — in three cases — reported to the Erasure Clerks by their own relatives.

**Phase 3 — The Descent (~Year 2,250):** Driven from the surface, the Vetha-moren migrated underground. Caldruun's cavern systems offered concealment, and something more — Vasuur's deep earth. Mineral deposits with unusual properties. Cave systems extending for hundreds of miles. Geothermal vents providing heat and energy.

**Phase 4 — The Divergence (~Years 2,300–2,400):** Underground life produced adaptations: skin darkened as melanin production shifted in response to mineral-rich water and low UV exposure. Build shifted toward denser musculature suited to confined spaces. Eyes adapted for low-light conditions. Some changes don't fit evolutionary timelines — developing across four or five generations, far too fast for natural selection. Two theories exist: Vasuur's subtle shaping (one of the few post-Second-Age divine interventions), or Eddan Tree residue (the Vetha-moren settled near the site of the third Eddan Tree — the roots extended deep, and trace divine energy in the soil may have accelerated divergence).

By Year 2,400, the split was irreversible. They called themselves **Morventhi** — "those who descend." Not passive. A declaration. They chose to go down. They chose to remember. The darkness that shaped them was, in their telling, the honest alternative to standing in the false light of a world built on a covered grave.

---

#### The Kethrun Undersprawl (Founded ~Year 2,350)

The Morventhi's first permanent underground city — **Kethrun** — was carved into a natural cavern system beneath central Caldruun. Engineering incorporated Rathai techniques and Gorundai mining tradition. A city built by three races' combined expertise, designed for a fourth.

**Layout:** Vertical, not horizontal. Seventeen major levels, each carved along natural geological strata. Upper levels house commerce and governance. Middle levels house residences. The lower levels — closest to the geothermal vents — house workshops, forges, and archives.

The archives are the point. The deepest level contains the **Vault of the Descent** — a library of oral and written records the Erasure Clerks have never reached. What's in the Vault includes Thalessi-perspective accounts of the coalition, partial copies of the Vaeluur Memoranda, and a genealogical record tracing Thalessi involvement in the genocide by family name. If this genealogy were ever made public, it would implicate several families that still hold power in modern Thalessi society — including the family of Mereth Solavane, Pillar of Commerce.

---

#### The Gorundai Fracture Period (Years ~2,000–3,100)

The Kelvosh Confederacy did not survive Druuga Kelvosh's death. Within a generation the confederation fractured along the same clan lines it had temporarily bridged. The result: roughly 1,100 years of inter-clan warfare on Caldruun.

**The Morrhgal Resurgence (~Year 2,200):** The Morrhgal clan — one of the three that had refused to participate in the genocide — rebuilt into a formidable force. Their moral authority grew as genocide details faded: being known as the clan that sheltered refugees carried weight. The Morrhgal became arbiters — the clan other clans called to settle disputes.

**The Slag Wars (~Years 2,500–2,600):** A century of conflict between seven clans over central Caldruun's mineral deposits. Produced the first Gorundai fighters retroactively classified as Paramount-tier.

Named Slag War figures:
- **Borrudan Keth** — Morrhgal clan champion, first Gorundai confirmed to have manifested King on a battlefield. His King erupted during the Siege of the Ashwell and dropped 600 opposing fighters unconscious. He never manifested it again. Died at sixty-nine without understanding what had happened.
- **Hassiv Gruuntash** — a smith-warrior who maintained a mobile forge on a war-cart and produced blades between engagements. Opposing clans called temporary ceasefires to commission pieces. Died peacefully at eighty-three, the wealthiest Gorundai of her century.
- **Draeven Tolchur** — warlord who united three clans into the Tolchur Pact, briefly controlled 40% of Caldruun's mining output, and was assassinated by his own lieutenant. The Pact dissolved overnight.

---

#### The First Sovereign King Claimants (Years ~2,300 onward)

The title "Sovereign King" did not begin as a formal designation. It began as a boast.

The earliest known claimant was **Kassava Duun** — a Human-Khari mixed-heritage navigator who, around Year 2,300, claimed to have sailed the full circumference of the world's oceans. Disputed but foundational — she NAMED the aspiration.

The informal definition by Year 3,000: someone who has sailed every known sea, visited every known continent, and returned alive. The "King" part is not gendered — three of the seven historical claimants were women.

**Historical claimants:**

1. **Kassava Duun (~Year 2,300)** — The first. Human-Khari. Navigational charts of the Bastion Sea used for centuries.

2. **Vorek Saalhune (~Year 2,700)** — Thalessi. Eighty years sailing. First with corroborated evidence. Did NOT reach the Sundren or Vorrenmaw.

3. **Duura Morrhgal (~Year 3,100)** — Gorundai. The first to frame the title as a challenge to authority. Sailed without Compact permission. Declared "the sea belongs to whoever can survive it." Spiritual ancestor of Renegade culture. First to attempt the Vorrenmaw approach — got within 200 miles of the Clutch. Returned with a compass that "pointed wrong" — toward the Vorrenmaw instead of north. Three hundred years later, the current Sovereign King carried a compass that did the same thing.

4. **Tennal Voss (~Year 3,800)** — Human. Fourth Age. First to explicitly defy the newly founded Masqarat. Highest bounty of his era: 400 million Sovereigns. Died at sea at fifty-one. Official record: "natural causes."

5. **Reshkha Gallacs (~Year 4,200)** — Oni. From the Sennaku Highlands. King-primary at Paramount tier. Carried the flags of eleven nations. Did not attempt the Vorrenmaw. "I know when the sea is telling me no." The Gallacs lineage ancestor of Tomoe Gallacs.

6. **Halvun Dossal (~Year 4,900)** — Rathai. 4'2", 90 pounds. Commanded a ship she'd designed herself. Her navigational data was so precise the Masqarat's cartography division adopted her charts wholesale. Distant descendant of Vekk Dossal. Died at 102.

7. **The current Sovereign King (~Year 5,550)** — The definitive holder. The one who reached the Vorrenmaw's heart. The one who has not come back. He carried Duura Morrhgal's compass. It pointed into the Vorrenmaw. He followed it.

---

#### The First Renegades (Years ~2,000 onward)

The *culture* of Renegade life — crews, bounties, codes, aesthetics — crystallized during the Third Age. The Compact's weakness was the Renegades' opportunity.

Three factors drove the transition from "outlaw" to "Renegade":

1. **The bounty system.** The Compact began issuing bounties around Year 2,100. The posters were public. The faces became famous. The first Renegade whose bounty poster was displayed as a point of pride: **Konneth Salve**, a Human smuggler who framed his poster above his ship's helm. "That number is what I'm worth to the people who couldn't catch me." Bounty: 800 Sovereigns.

2. **Grimoire amplification.** The network allowed Renegade exploits and bounty information to spread faster than the Compact could control. The Compact tried to restrict Renegade-related content. This made the content more popular.

3. **The Morrhgal tradition.** Duura Morrhgal's framing of the Sovereign King title as defiance connected exploration with resistance. The Renegade code inherited her philosophy: the sea belongs to whoever can survive it.

**Named Third Age Renegade legends:**
- **Konneth Salve (~Year 2,150)** — First bounty-poster displayer. Human. 800 Sovereigns. Died at forty-three, in bed, surrounded by his crew.
- **Suura Denn (~Year 2,400)** — Khari captain. Ran unlicensed trade routes using Path-Hider routes. "Denn Route" still means "uncharted shortcut" in modern Bastion Sea slang. Distant descendant of Kassha Denn.
- **Garrah Voss (~Year 2,700)** — Human. First classified as an "existential threat" by the Compact. Held territory for forty years. Fleet of thirty ships. Armoring at low Paramount tier. Died of a heart attack at sixty-two during a feast.

---

#### The Masqarat's Founding (~Year 2,800)

By Year 2,800, the Compact was failing at its public mission. Mettavuur made a calculation: the Compact needed to become something stronger, or the Erasure infrastructure would collapse with it.

The **Masqarat** was founded in Year 2,803. Not a new institution — a restructured one:
- **Permanent capital** established on Orenthas
- **Centralized military authority** — the proto-Wardensea
- **The Four Pillars system** — Enforcement, Commerce, Silence, Law
- **The hidden sovereign formalized** — Mettavuur withdrew from all public-facing roles. Handwritten orders in Silverborn script, delivered by courier.
- **The bounty system professionalized** — standardized in Sovereigns, issued through official channels

The mortal leaders who negotiated the restructuring did not know they were negotiating with a 2,200-year-old Silverborn genocide architect. They were building exactly what Mettavuur needed: a government strong enough to maintain the lie, structured so no mortal could ever find the person holding the strings.

---

### THE FOURTH AGE (~Years 3,400–5,540): The Age of Institutions and Blood

#### The Vorrenmaw Pulse (Year 3,398)

For two days, the Vorrenmaw's spin accelerated to roughly three times its baseline rate. The Clutch expanded outward by fifty miles. The Howling Gyre generated storms that reached the southern Bastion Sea. Twelve ships lost. The amber cast above the Vorrenmaw brightened to a visible glow reported by crews 500 miles away.

Then it stopped. The spin returned to normal.

The hidden sovereign's directive, issued three days later: *"Increase perimeter patrol. Double rotation. The seal holds. Nothing has changed. Do not investigate further."*

The phrase "the seal holds" is the only time Mettavuur has ever referenced the Vorrenmaw's contents in a written directive. Something had tested the seal. It had held. But the fact that it was tested at all meant the situation was not stable.

---

#### The Conqueror Wars (Years ~3,500–4,200)

The Fourth Age's defining feature on Caldruun: fighters who exceeded Paramount tier and began reshaping geography.

**The first recognized Conqueror-class fighter: Vashenne Morghul** (~Year 3,500). Gorundai. Morrhgal lineage. King measured at levels that "exceeded the scale." Held a third of Caldruun for forty-one years. Fought the Masqarat's expansion forces to a standstill three times. She is the reason the Masqarat developed the **Doctrine of Attrition** — "No one fights a Conqueror alone. You fight them with numbers, attrition, and time. You accept losses. You outlast them."

Vashenne died at ninety, still holding her territory. Her death triggered the **Morghul Succession** — three Conqueror-tier fighters competing for her domain, scarring central Caldruun with blast craters and geological fractures. The modern lake basins in central Caldruun? The Morghul Succession filled them.

**Named Conqueror-era figures:**
- **Torren Duuvahn (~3,590-3,650)** — Human. Law-type God Fruit (froze kinetic energy). Combined with Paramount-tier Armoring, essentially immovable. Held a mountain pass for forty years. The pass is still called **Duuvahn's Gate.**
- **Kaellith Varanduur (~3,600-3,710)** — Thalessi. No God Fruit. Longest-lived Conqueror in history (~400 years old). Didn't want territory — wanted to be left alone. Established a domain on a Bastion Sea island and sank seven Masqarat expeditions. Died of natural causes (the only Conqueror in history to do so). Built an observatory and spent her final decades watching the Vorrenmaw's amber glow. Her notes were classified.

---

#### The Masqarat Expansion Wars (Years ~3,800–4,300)

The **Wardensea** was formally established around Year 3,850 — separate from regional garrisons, professionally trained, centrally commanded. The white coat tradition began here: first officers wore undyed linen coats. The symbolism came later.

**The Rekkvald Campaign (~Year 3,900):** Northern coastal clans formed the **Rekkvald Compact** in direct response to Masqarat expansion. Refused integration, maintained their own naval tradition, and negotiated alliance instead of subjugation. The Masqarat accepted because the Rekkvald's fleet was too good to fight and too useful to lose.

**The Kethrun Siege (~Year 4,000):** Three-year siege of the Morventhi Undersprawl. The Masqarat couldn't navigate the tunnel network. The Morventhi inflicted casualties at a 15:1 ratio. The Masqarat withdrew and offered autonomy.

**The Sennaku Pacification (~Year 4,100):** The Masqarat committed three fleet divisions and 40,000 soldiers. Lost 12,000 in eighteen months. Abandoned. The Sennaku Highlands remain the world's most prominent example of successful resistance — and Kuldhara Mabaan's current stronghold is the same territory Setsuda defended.

---

#### The Fourth Age Deep Bench

**Named Fourth Age Conquerors (chronological):**
- **Vashenne Morghul** — First recognized Conqueror. Gorundai.
- **Torren Duuvahn** — Human. Law-type God Fruit. Mountain pass.
- **Kaellith Varanduur** — Thalessi. Observatory. Natural causes.
- **Reshkha Gallacs (~Year 4,200)** — Oni. Sovereign King claimant. Gallacs lineage ancestor.
- **Daethorn Savuul (~Year 4,400)** — Morventhi. First underground Conqueror — held territory in the Undersprawl's outer tunnel networks. Never fought on the surface. Died at 371, having held his territory for 190 years.
- **Orussa Venn (~Year 4,800)** — Human. Beast-type God Fruit (prehistoric raptor form). Most destructive Fourth Age Conqueror. Three major island chains now uninhabited because of her rampages. Killed by two Paramounts and a fleet of forty ships. Bounty at death: 2.1 billion Sovereigns — highest pre-Great-Renegade-Era bounty.
- **Ghennet Halvess (~Year 5,200)** — Human. Rekkvald origin. Ancestor of Sigrund Halvess. A Conqueror who retired — negotiated surrender for amnesty and a governance position within the Rekkvald Compact. Renegade culture considers it betrayal. The Rekkvald consider it pragmatism. Sigrund's authority derives from a Conqueror grandfather who chose peace.

---

#### The Thrallic Reformation (~Year 5,384)

For over three thousand years, Gorundai culture was defined by clan warfare. **Thrall Goruven** changed this.

Born to a minor clan on eastern Caldruun — the Goruven, a fishing and herding community with no military distinction. He was not a warrior. He was a mediator — a role that Gorundai culture considered approximately one step above "coward."

Thrall's innovation was simple: he proposed that three neighboring clans resolve a territorial dispute through structured negotiation instead of war. The negotiation took eleven days. No one died. The neighboring clans were shocked — not because negotiation was unknown, but because a Gorundai had proposed it voluntarily. This wasn't an outsider telling the Gorundai to stop fighting. This was an Orc saying: "We can do better than this."

Thrall spent forty years repeating the process. Clan by clan. Dispute by dispute. He was challenged to honor-combat fourteen times and declined every one, which should have destroyed his reputation but instead created a new category: the Gorundai who chose a harder fight.

**Impact:** The clan system transitioned from competitive to cooperative. "Communal honor" replaced "individual honor." Gorundai smithwork experienced a renaissance. Orc representation in the Masqarat expanded — Heskan Druuvedt, the current Pillar of Law, is a direct product. Thrall died at seventy-nine, beloved by most, resented by some. The Reformation was not complete — elements of old warrior culture persist, particularly in western Caldruun.

---

#### The Kolmari Rise (~Years 4,500–5,000)

Economic power coalesced in the **Kolmari Trade Confederation** — a consortium of Thalessi and Human merchant houses.

**Sellareth Kolmas (~Year 4,500)** — Thalessi merchant-scholar who proposed the first standardized credit instrument: the **Kolmari Note**, a written promise redeemable at any affiliated trading post. Eliminated the need to carry physical Sovereigns on sea voyages. Lived to 380.

**Durran Okasch (~Year 4,600)** — Human banker who introduced "managed risk" — lending to Renegade crews with the expectation that a percentage would default but successful returns would cover losses. Made the Kolmari the financial backbone of the Renegade economy. Every Renegade crew that buys supplies on credit is using Okasch's system.

---

#### The Vethara and Gannavar Foundings

**Vethara** — established during the early Third Age (~Year 2,000), modern form formalized through Masqarat treaty around Year 3,200. A semi-autonomous city-state with its own legal code and educational system. Mereth Solavane, current Pillar of Commerce, is Vetharan.

**Gannavar** — the Varrek city-state, founded around Year 3,500 as a center of study and engineering. The Grimoire network was developed here. First functional prototype around Year 4,300.

---

#### The End of the Fourth Age — Year 5,540

The Fourth Age ends with an event that will eventually be recognized as the defining moment of the current era:

**The Sovereign King reaches the Vorrenmaw.**

Born Year 5,529 on Orenthas. Discovered the Sundren at twenty-one (Year 5,550). Completed a voyage touching every known sea and continent at thirty-three (Year 5,562). At thirty-four, carrying Duura Morrhgal's compass, he entered the Vorrenmaw.

He has not come back. That was twenty-two years ago.

His rival — still alive — responded to his disappearance by doing something that ignited the Great Renegade Era. The fuse was lit. The Fifth Age began.

The transition from Fourth to Fifth Age is unique in this world's history: triggered not by a catastrophe, a war, or an institutional shift, but by a single person's absence. The Sovereign King left a hole. The world is still falling into it.

---

## 6. THE EIGHT RACES + CULTURE

### Humans — 45% of world population

**Physiology:** Standard. No innate biological advantages. Lifespan: 70-85 years.
**Distribution:** Everywhere. Majority on Orenthas, plurality on Caldruun, bulk of Masqarat administrative class. Wardensea approximately 60% human.
**Dominion Tendency:** No racial lean. Vision, Armoring, and King in roughly proportional numbers. Ceiling is purely individual.

**Cultural Role:** Humans dominate through numbers, institutional control, and adaptability. They didn't build the Masqarat alone, but they run it. The Four Pillars are — as far as anyone knows — all human.

**Naming:** Regional variation is extreme. Orenthan humans favor longer, vowel-heavy surnames (Gallacs, Oumachi, Solavane). Bastion Sea humans trend shorter (Goss, Ossk, Saul). Caldruun humans near Gorundai territory borrow consonant clusters (Khorasst, Duulross). A human's name tells you where their family settled three generations ago.

**Key Holidays:** **The Founding Fast** (3 days, spring) — Day one: no food dawn to dusk, commemorating the "hunger years" before centralized governance. Day two: communal feast, every household contributes one dish. Day three: market day where debts are settled. Renegade crews observe it ironically — fasting "for the government that wants us dead." **The Lantern Tide** (1 night, autumn equinox) — coastal communities launch small candle-boats carrying the names of the dead. Older than the Masqarat, older than organized worship.

**Death Customs:** Sea burial or earth burial. Coastal tradition includes a **name-reading**: full name, parents' names, one sentence describing what the person did that mattered. Archived in the local Masqarat office. Thalveris contains 4.6 million name-readings spanning 800 years.

**Marriage:** Legal contract filed with the nearest Masqarat office. Bastion Sea Renegades who want to marry: ship's captain officiates, registered at the next friendly port.

*"They breed fast, organize faster, and forget fastest."*

---


#### Humanization — Humans: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Humans**

A human harbor clerk in Thalveris wakes to the Grimoire alarm that six hundred million other humans across Orenthas wake to — a standardized tone that a Varrek designed and a Rathai manufactured and that no one likes and no one changes because the alternative is being late. Her apartment is on the fourth floor of a stone-and-timber building in the Dockside Quarter, two rooms, a shared privy down the hall, a window facing the harbor that she chose because the sound of ships is, for a Thalveris native, the sound of the world working. Rent is four Silver Spans a month, which is thirty percent of her salary, which is standard and slightly unfair and has been standard and slightly unfair for as long as anyone remembers.

Breakfast is bread and hard cheese from the market on Lowergate Street, eaten while walking because she is always nearly late and has accepted this as a character trait rather than a solvable problem. The walk takes twelve minutes through streets that smell of salt, tar, fish guts from the processing district, and the particular chemical sharpness of Grimoire relay towers warming up for the day's transmission load. She passes humans, mostly — Thalveris is sixty-five percent human — but the docks are mixed. Rathai cable technicians on their way to the relay stations. A Gorundai stevedore who nods at her every morning and whose name she has never learned despite three years of mutual nodding. Two Khari traders setting up a market stall with the efficient speed that makes other races feel like they're moving through water.

The harbor clerk's office is a room with a desk, a filing system that predates her by forty years, and a window that looks out over the busiest port in the Bastion Sea. Her job is logging arrivals: ship name, captain, crew size, cargo manifest, port of origin, and the subtle assessment — never written, always remembered — of whether the captain is lying about any of the above. She logs between thirty and fifty ships per day. She recognizes roughly a third of them by sight. The merchants are predictable. The Renegades are not. A Renegade crew that docks at Thalveris is either resupplying with a low profile or about to cause a problem, and the harbor clerk's job is to figure out which before the Wardensea garrison does, because the Wardensea's response to uncertainty is a patrol boat and the patrol boat scares off the merchants who pay the actual bills.

Midday she eats at the desk — a tavern meal delivered by a boy who runs food for the Lowergate vendors, soup and bread and a slice of something that might be meat. Afternoon is more logging, a meeting with the port authority that accomplishes nothing, and the slow awareness that the day is ending not because the work is done but because the light through the window is changing. She leaves at dusk. The evening is her own: a tavern near her building where she drinks one ale, checks her Grimoire for news (the Broadwave's evening bounty announcements are a communal event — the tavern goes quiet, the Grimoire volume goes up, and thirty humans sit together watching someone else's poster and making judgments they'll never be asked for), and walks home to the sound of the harbor settling into its night-pattern. Ships creaking. Water against stone. A Wardensea patrol's running lights tracing a slow arc across the bay. She falls asleep to all of it — the noise of a city that has not stopped in eight hundred years and has no intention of starting now.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Humans**

Humans built the Masqarat. Not alone — the Thalessi designed the legal architecture, the Varrek engineered the infrastructure, the Gorundai provided the military muscle — but the administrative class that runs the institution day to day is sixty percent human, and the cultural assumptions embedded in the system are human assumptions. The Founding Fast holiday commemorates the "hunger years" before centralized governance, and every human child learns the story: before the Masqarat, there was chaos. The Masqarat brought order. Order brought food. Food brought civilization. The narrative is clean, satisfying, and the foundation of human institutional identity.

The same humans who celebrate the Masqarat as civilization's guarantor are the humans who fill the Renegade ranks. The Wardensea is sixty percent human. The Renegade crews are roughly forty percent human. The bounty posters that cover every harbor wall feature human faces in plurality. The race that built the system is also the race most vigorously trying to break it. This is not a schism between two populations — it is often the same family. A Wardensea Lieutenant whose younger brother runs with a Renegade crew is not a dramatic edge case. It is a demographic regularity. Human families split across the institutional divide the way other families split across professional lines.

The contradiction runs deeper than politics. Humans pride themselves on adaptability — the ability to thrive anywhere, fit any role, learn any skill. This is accurate. It is also the reason humans have no fixed cultural identity in the way that the Gorundai have forge-culture or the Thalessi have archive-culture. A human from Thalveris has more in common, culturally, with the Thalessi who live there than with a human from the Sennaku Highlands. A Bastion Sea human shares more values with Khari trading partners than with a Orenthan farmer. Human adaptability is human dissolution — the ability to become anything is the inability to be something specific, and the human search for identity (through the Masqarat, through Renegade culture, through the Kingsrun, through sheer ambition) is the search for the fixed point that human biology refuses to provide. Every other race has an answer to "what are we?" The human answer is "it depends," and the restlessness that generates is the engine that drives half the world's conflicts and most of its progress.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Humans**

What humans say about other races depends entirely on which humans you ask, which is itself the most human answer possible.

A Thalveris bureaucrat: the Thalessi are useful but insufferable, the Gorundai are reliable but frightening, the Rathai are essential but forgettable, and the Morventhi are fascinating but untrustworthy. These opinions are held simultaneously, expressed selectively, and revised based on the most recent interaction. A human who distrusted Gorundai yesterday will trust one today because the specific Gorundai they met was good company. Humans do not hold racial prejudices the way long-lived races do — as settled, centuries-deep convictions. Human prejudice is shallow, mutable, and responsive to anecdote over data. This makes it less poisonous and less predictable. A Thalessi's prejudice against Morventhi will not change in their lifetime. A human's prejudice against anyone might change by Tuesday.

The one consistent current: humans are uncomfortable with races that live longer than they do. The Thalessi, the Morventhi, the Lunarians — any race whose lifespan dwarfs the human maximum triggers a specific anxiety that humans rarely articulate and always carry. The sense that these races have seen more, know more, and are making decisions based on contexts that a human will never live long enough to understand. The response to this anxiety splits predictably: some humans defer to the long-lived (the Masqarat's institutional hierarchy reflects this — Thalessi and Morventhi hold disproportionate advisory positions), and some humans resent them (the Renegade movement's emotional core is partly the refusal to accept that someone else's longer view should override your shorter one). Both responses are human. Both are simultaneous. Both make the Thalessi tired.

What other races say about humans: "There are so many of them." The first observation. The second: "They change so fast." A Thalessi who negotiated a treaty with a human government returns fifty years later to find the government replaced, the treaty ignored, and a new generation asking "who are you?" The human capacity for institutional amnesia — for forgetting what was agreed to, who agreed to it, and why — is, for long-lived races, the most frustrating human trait. The humans who remember are the ones who write things down. The humans who don't are the ones who make history by ignoring it.

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Humans**

Humans fall in love the way humans do everything: variably, messily, and without a unifying cultural framework to guide the process. A Thalveris human falls differently than a Bastion Sea human, who falls differently than a Caldruun human, and the absence of a single human courtship tradition is itself the most human thing about human love. There is no human equivalent of thrakkenvoss, or archive tea, or the Morventhi's direct declaration. There is just the specific person, the specific moment, and whatever they do with it.

What unifies human romantic experience is the urgency. Seventy to eighty-five years. The Thalessi can take a decade to decide. The Gorundai can take months to resolve their feelings through competitive exercise. Humans cannot afford the wait. A human who is attracted to someone and waits too long carries the awareness, conscious or not, that the window is limited and every month spent hesitating is a month of a lifespan they don't get back. This produces the full range of human romantic behavior: the impulsive declaration, the slow burn that accelerates suddenly, the regrettable decision at a harbor tavern, the long-term partnership built from shared commutes and accumulated small kindnesses. All of these are human love. None of them is the definitive version.

Human grief is the same: variable, personal, and without a cultural template that reliably applies. The coastal tradition — the name-reading, the one sentence describing what the person did that mattered — is the closest thing to a universal human mourning practice, and it varies by region, by family, by the specific weight of the specific loss. A human who loses a partner in Thalveris might observe the name-reading, light a candle at the Lantern Tide, and drink with friends until the initial numbness resolves into the longer, duller ache that humans call "getting through it." A human who loses a partner on a Renegade ship might not have a body to bury, a port to mourn in, or a community to hold the space, and the grief is carried forward on a moving vessel where the work doesn't stop and the ocean doesn't care.

The thing that humans carry longest, across all regions and traditions, is the sentence. The name-reading's single sentence — "what the person did that mattered" — is the human equivalent of the Gorundai soul-bar or the Thalessi ceramic vessel. It compresses a life into a claim, and the choosing of that sentence is, for the family that does it, the most painful part of the funeral. Not the burial. Not the ceremony. The sentence. Because the sentence is the last thing the world will know about this person, and seventy-five years of living must fit into twelve words, and twelve words is not enough, and it is all there is.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Humans**

Human children are loud, creative, imitative, and everywhere. A Thalveris street at midday contains more children per square foot than any equivalent space in a Thalessi city, a Varrek city, or any long-lived race's settlement, because humans reproduce faster, reach maturity faster, and produce more children per family — two to four is standard, five is common in rural and maritime communities. The sheer density of human children in urban environments produces a childhood culture that is, uniquely among the races, self-generating. Human children teach each other. The games, the songs, the rules of engagement for a street-fight — all of it is transmitted child-to-child, bypassing adult instruction entirely, in a parallel culture that reforms itself every generation.

The games are regional and innumerable. Thalveris harbor children play **dock-chase**, which is exactly what it sounds like — a running game across the harbor's wooden platforms that ends when someone falls in the water, which is both the losing condition and the most entertaining outcome for everyone else. Rural Orenthan children play variations of catch-and-throw with whatever's available. Bastion Sea children on island settlements play **tide-race** — running across exposed reef at low tide, trying to reach the farthest point before the water returns. Adults discourage this. Children ignore the discouragement. The scars from tide-race are, in Bastion Sea communities, a marker of childhood that adults reference decades later.

What they get in trouble for: everything, depending on the parent. Human child-rearing has no racial template. A strict Thalveris household and a permissive Bastion Sea household will produce children who were punished for different things, praised for different things, and who will, as adults, argue about which upbringing was correct with a passion that suggests the argument is really about something else. The closest thing to a universal human parental phrase is some variation of "because I said so" — the admission that the parent has no principled justification for the rule and is relying on authority alone. Every human child hears this. Every human child recognizes it as the moment the parent's reasoning failed. Every human parent knows the child recognizes it. The phrase survives anyway because the alternative — explaining everything to a five-year-old — takes longer than the human lifespan allows.

The human rite of passage has no single form. In Thalveris, it's the first job. On the Bastion Sea, it's the first solo voyage. In rural Orenthas, it's the first harvest the child manages alone. On a Renegade ship, it's the first fight — not a formal challenge like the Gorundai's First Challenge, just the first time the situation demands that the youngest crew member step up and the crew watches to see what happens. Human adulthood arrives not through ceremony but through event — the unplanned moment where a child acts like an adult and someone notices. The noticing is the rite. The event varies. The recognition is always the same: a look from an older human that says, without words, "you're one of us now." The look is the ceremony, and it is enough, because humans have never needed much ceremony. They have needed a lot of courage. The courage is the human rite of passage, and it is never the same twice.

---

### Gorundai (Orcs) — 15% of world population

**Physiology:** Larger than humans by a head. Denser musculature, thicker bone structure, tusked lower jaw, skin tones from deep green to grey-brown. Lifespan: 70-85 years. Physically mature faster.
**Distribution:** Concentrated in central and eastern Caldruun. Significant diaspora in Orenthan ports and Merchant Spine islands. Growing presence in Sundren expedition camps.
**Dominion Tendency:** Strong Armoring lean. King users rare but historically devastating.

**Cultural Role:** Defined by the **Thrallic Reformation** (~200 years ago). Post-Reformation Gorundai value communal honor, direct speech, and craftsmanship. Their smithwork is the best in the world.

**Naming:** Front-loaded — strong opening consonants, often monosyllabic first names with longer clan surnames. Clan names persist even post-Reformation. Dropping one is considered erasure, not freedom.

**Food:** Calorie-dense, protein-heavy. **Gorrash** — fermented grain porridge that tastes like punishment and fuels a full day's march. Nobody likes it. Everybody eats it. Feast culture is the exception: whole-roasted game, spiced bone-marrow served in the skull, and **the clan pot** — a massive iron cauldron where every attendee adds one ingredient. The resulting stew is unpredictable, always too salty, and consumed with aggressive enthusiasm.

**Key Holidays:** **The Reckoning Fire** (1 night, autumn) — grievances written on leather strips, thrown into bonfire. Stories told until dawn — must be funny. **The Moot-Mark** (1 day, spring, Confederation only) — re-reads the Reformation charter. Holdout clans observe the same day as mourning.

**Coming-of-Age:** The **First Challenge** at fifteen — one-on-one combat with an older clan member. Expected to lose. What matters is how.

**Death Customs:** Cremation on pyres of personal possessions. Ashes mixed with iron dust, pressed into a **soul-bar** kept by the family and eventually incorporated into a weapon or tool. The cycle of metal is literal.

**Marriage:** Sealed with a **shared scar** — each partner cuts the other's palm with a clan knife, hands pressed together. Displayed openly.

*"Build like siege engines, argue like lawyers, and hold a grudge like it pays interest."*

---


#### Humanization — Gorundai: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Gorundai**

A Gorundai ironworker in Duunvedt wakes with the furnace shift-horn at fourth-bell — still dark, the eastern sky over the Ironmarrow Coast showing nothing yet but the red underglow of the smelting district. She eats gorrash standing up, the same way her mother ate it and her mother's mother before that, spooned from a clay bowl that she fires herself every few years when the old one cracks. The porridge is awful. It has always been awful. She eats every grain because the furnace shift runs nine hours and a Gorundai who faints at the bellows shames the family name for a season. Her boots are iron-soled — not for fashion but because the slag-fields between the housing blocks and the fissure forges will melt leather in the hot months. She walks the same path every morning, past the soul-bar maker's shopfront where old Tessik is already grinding iron-ash into the day's first ingots. They nod. Tessik nods back. Fourteen years of this greeting, wordless, reliable as tide. At the forge gate she joins thirty others, all of them checking the seam-board — a slate wall where the shift boss chalks the day's quotas and, underneath in smaller script, which forge threw sparks yesterday and why. Someone always threw sparks yesterday. The argument about whose fault it was will carry through the morning, get louder after the midday meal of salt-crusted goat and root mash served on the furnace stones, and resolve itself either through consensus or — if consensus fails — a brief, violent grapple behind the cooling shed that the shift boss pretends not to see. She'll be home by late afternoon. Her daughter will have left a bowl of something on the step — the girl is twelve and learning to cook, which means the food is unpredictable but always earnest. The ironworker will eat it, compliment what she can, correct what she must, and spend the evening resharpening her forge-tongs because a Gorundai does not bring dull tools to a morning shift. She falls asleep to the low, constant vibration of the deep-fissure vents beneath the city — a sound so steady that Duunvedt-born Gorundai who move away report insomnia for months, missing a heartbeat in the stone they'd stopped noticing was there.

---


**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Gorundai**

The Gorundai say they left the old ways behind with the Thrallic Reformation. They say the Reformation replaced blood-rule with communal honor, that the Great Moot speaks for every clan equally, and that no Gorundai bows to strength alone anymore. They believe this. They teach it to their children. They recite the Reformation charter every spring at the Moot-Mark and feel genuine pride in what their grandparents built.

Then they elect the biggest fighter in the room to lead the local council. Every time. Not by rule — by instinct. A Gorundai who speaks well but cannot fight will be respected as an advisor, consulted on craft matters, and quietly passed over when the community needs someone to stand at the front. The Confederation's three regional governors are all former warriors. The holdout clans mock this as proof the Reformation changed nothing. The Confederation dismisses the accusation and then promotes another combat veteran to a leadership post. Nobody acknowledges the pattern because acknowledging it would mean the Reformation didn't fix what it claimed to fix — and the Reformation is the thing that separates modern Gorundai from the warlord culture that almost destroyed them. So they don't look at it directly. They just keep voting for the person with the most scars and calling it merit.

There is a second, quieter contradiction. Gorundai culture prizes directness — say what you mean, no hedging, no politeness for its own sake. A Gorundai who flatters is distrusted. A Gorundai who lies is ostracized. But every Gorundai carries at least one thing they will not speak aloud. The soul-bar in the family shrine — the iron-pressed ashes of the dead — is treated with absolute silence. You do not discuss whose ashes are in which bar. You do not ask how many bars a family holds. You do not comment on the age or condition of the shrine. A race that prides itself on saying everything has built its most sacred tradition around the one subject nobody will touch. The dead are honored loudly at the Reckoning Fire, with stories told until dawn. But the soul-bars themselves — the physical remains — sit in their alcoves in permanent quiet, and a Gorundai home that has too many of them carries a grief that no amount of directness will articulate.

---


**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Gorundai**

What Gorundai say about other races when no outsiders are listening:

Humans are soft. Not weak — soft. They hedge. They negotiate when they should decide. They smile when they're angry and call it diplomacy. A Gorundai respects a human who throws a punch more than a human who writes a letter, and this is unfair and they know it's unfair and they say it anyway. The exception is maritime humans — Bastion Sea sailors who work hard, drink hard, and settle disagreements with their hands. Those humans are tolerable. "Almost Gorundai," which is the highest compliment a Gorundai gives and one that every human finds mildly insulting.

Thalessi are exhausting. They talk beautifully and say nothing for three hundred years. A Gorundai clan leader who has to negotiate a trade agreement with a Thalessi delegation will, by tradition, assign their most patient member to the task, then drink with the rest of the council while they wait. The wait is always longer than estimated. Gorundai respect Thalessi craftsmanship — their archive tea is grudgingly acknowledged as superior to anything a Gorundai kitchen produces — but the idea of spending six months courting someone before kissing them is considered a form of psychological torture.

Morventhi get a nod. Underground builders, hard workers, meritocratic. The closest cultural cousins the Gorundai will admit to having. The friction is religious: Morventhi carve Vasuur glyphs into their support beams; Gorundai invoke Vasuur as "Vasra" in forge-songs. Both sides claim the older tradition. Neither will yield. The argument has been running for two thousand years and has generated exactly zero progress and roughly four hundred tavern brawls.

Rathai are respected and pitied. They build everything, get paid nothing, and live in boxes. A Gorundai who visits the Nettleworks comes home shaking their head. "Four people in a room that size. How do they breathe?" The Gorundai admire Rathai stubbornness — it mirrors their own — but find Rathai efficiency unsettling. A Gorundai takes pride in the hours a thing requires. A Rathai takes pride in reducing those hours. The philosophies are incompatible and both sides know it.

Oni are kin. Not culturally — the Sennaku Highlands Oni and the Caldruun Gorundai have different traditions, different gods, different everything. But the body recognizes the body. Gorundai and Oni drink together easily, fight together naturally, and understand each other's volume. The friction is subtle: Gorundai think Oni are reckless. Oni think Gorundai are rigid. Both are correct. Neither will admit the other has a point.

Varrek are useful and baffling. A Gorundai smith works metal by feel — by the color of the heat, the ring of the hammer, the way the grain sits. A Varrek engineer measures the same metal with six instruments and writes a paper about it. Both produce excellent results. The Gorundai cannot understand why the Varrek need the instruments. The Varrek cannot understand how the Gorundai don't. This mutual incomprehension is, paradoxically, the foundation of the best cross-racial professional partnerships in the world. Gorundai-Varrek forge teams produce Master-grade Resonance weapons. Neither side understands the other's contribution. Both know they need it.

Khari are liked individually and distrusted collectively. A Khari trader who visits a Gorundai market is welcome — good goods, fair prices, interesting news. But Khari warrens that pass through Gorundai territory without stopping are watched. Nomads who don't settle make Gorundai uncomfortable for reasons they can't fully articulate. The Gorundai are builders. They put things in the ground and they stay. A people who carry everything and belong to no fixed place challenges something fundamental about how the Gorundai understand the world.

Lunarians are ghosts. The Gorundai have almost no contact with Lunarian communities and prefer it that way. Not hostility — discomfort. Something about the glow, the silence, the sense that they know things they aren't saying. A Gorundai who meets a Lunarian will be polite, finish the interaction as quickly as possible, and spend the evening feeling like someone read a page of their life they hadn't offered.

What other races say about the Gorundai: "Don't make them angry" is the first thing. This exhausts the Gorundai more than anything else. They have spent two hundred years reforming their culture, building institutions, producing the finest metalwork in the world, and the first thing every outsider thinks is "big, angry, dangerous." The Gorundai joke about it. "I could forge you a blade that sings, but you'd rather hear about the time my grandfather hit someone." The joke doesn't hide the bruise. They are tired of being the world's cautionary tale about uncontrolled strength when what they actually are is a civilization that chose, deliberately and at great cost, to control itself.

---


**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Gorundai**

A nervous Gorundai is the loudest person in the room. This is counterintuitive — the race prizes directness, so you'd expect them to simply say what they feel. But desire isn't a forge. It doesn't respond to hammering. A Gorundai who's attracted to someone will overcompensate in every direction: talking too much, laughing at things that aren't funny, offering to carry things the other person can clearly carry themselves, and — most tellingly — picking fights with anyone nearby to demonstrate value. This last behavior is so common that Gorundai communities have a word for it: **thrakkenvoss**, roughly "showing your tusks at the wrong crowd." A Gorundai's friends can identify the object of their interest within hours, because the smitten Gorundai will be pointedly not looking at that person while doing increasingly absurd things in their peripheral vision.

The actual approach, when it comes, is blunt. Gorundai courtship lasts three to five encounters — a shared meal, a shared task, a shared fight, and if all three feel right, a direct statement of intent that leaves no room for misinterpretation. A Gorundai who has been circling someone for two weeks and then says "I want you" is not being crude. They are being brave. The two weeks of thrakkenvoss were the hard part. The declaration is the relief.

What makes Gorundai love different from the outside perception is the tenderness that follows the bluntness. A Gorundai partner remembers what you eat, fixes the things you own before you notice they're broken, and shows up when the work is worst. Grand gestures are rare. Quiet consistency is everything. The shared scar of marriage is not the peak of Gorundai romance — it's the seal on something that was already built through a hundred mornings of wordless reliability. A Gorundai in love is a Gorundai who has decided you are part of the structure of their life, and they will maintain you with the same stubborn care they give their forge-tongs and their family shrine.

Gorundai grief is architectural. It builds.

The first hour after a death is noise — a sound that non-Gorundai find startling. Wailing, open and uncontrolled, from everyone present. Gorundai do not believe in private grief. The dead deserve volume. The sound carries through Gorundai settlements like a signal, and neighbors who hear it stop what they're doing, put down their tools, and wait. Not to help — there is nothing to help yet. To witness. A Gorundai death is a community event from the first breath after the last breath.

The first day is work. The body is prepared by the family. The pyre is built by the community — everyone contributes one piece of wood or one personal possession of the dead. The soul-bar maker is called. The ashes will be mixed with iron dust and pressed while still warm, because a soul-bar made from cold ashes is considered incomplete, the spirit not fully caught.

The first week is the Telling. Every person who knew the dead tells one story. The stories must be funny. This is not optional. At the Reckoning Fire, the rule applies to the community's collective dead. At a private funeral, it applies to the individual. A Gorundai who cannot find something funny to say about the dead person is considered to have not known them well enough to grieve them, which is perhaps the harshest quiet judgment in Gorundai culture.

A year later, the grief has gone underground in the way Gorundai grief always does — into the body, into the work. A grieving Gorundai works harder. Forges longer. Sleeps less. Their hands stay busy because idle hands remember. The soul-bar sits in the family shrine, and the grieving Gorundai will not touch it for the first year. After the first year, they may hold it during the Reckoning Fire. After the second year, they may speak to it. These are not rules. Nobody teaches them. Every Gorundai knows.

The thing they never talk about: the weight of the shrine. A family that has lost many members has a shrine heavy with soul-bars. The iron accumulates. Older families — those who survived the pre-Reformation wars, the Fourth Age Conqueror clashes, the mine collapses and the sea crossings — have shrines that a single person cannot lift. When a Gorundai family moves, the shrine goes first, carried by as many hands as needed, in silence, through whatever street or road or gangplank lies between the old home and the new. Passersby step aside without being asked. The weight tells the story. No one needs to hear it spoken.

---


**E. The Children Paragraph — Gorundai**

Gorundai children are loud, physical, and constantly testing the structural integrity of their surroundings. A Gorundai toddler does not crawl — they barrel. By three, they're climbing whatever is tallest. By five, they're wrestling anyone within arm's reach, including adults who didn't volunteer. Gorundai parenting philosophy is straightforward: let them fall, let them fight, let them figure it out. A Gorundai parent will watch their child tumble off a wall, wait to see if anything is broken, and if not, say "again?" This is not cruelty. It is the bone-deep conviction that a child who learns what hurts is a child who learns what matters.

The games are violent by any other race's standards. **Thrakkha** — a team game played with a leather ball stuffed with sand, roughly the size of a human head — has no formal rules. The objective, in theory, is to get the ball to a designated stone. In practice, it is to get the ball away from whoever has it through any means short of weapons. Children play thrakkha from age six. Injuries are constant and worn as credentials. A Gorundai child's social status among peers is determined almost entirely by thrakkha performance until the age of twelve, at which point forge apprenticeships begin and a new hierarchy based on craft skill slowly overlays the old one. Some children never fully make the transition. The best thrakkha player in a Gorundai district carries an authority among the young that no elder appointment can match.

What they get in trouble for: breaking things. Not each other — each other is expected. Objects. Tools. Walls. A Gorundai child who breaks a forge tool will hear about it for a year, because tools represent work and work represents the family's survival. The phrase every Gorundai child hears and every Gorundai child rolls their eyes at: **"Vusha threnn keth duunga"** — roughly, "The iron doesn't care how you feel." Meaning: your emotions are valid but the work doesn't wait, the tool won't fix itself, and your feelings are not an excuse for damaging something the family needs.
