Every Gorundai adult can recite it from memory. Every Gorundai adult also admits, privately, that they didn't understand what it meant until they were at least twenty-five.

The rite of passage is the First Challenge at fifteen — one-on-one combat with an older clan member, usually an aunt or uncle, occasionally a parent if the family believes the child needs a harder lesson. The child is expected to lose. This is not a test of strength. It is a test of response to failure. A child who loses and stands back up is ready. A child who loses and rages is told to come back next season. A child who loses and cries is comforted, told it's natural, and brought back the following week — because Gorundai culture does not consider crying a weakness, only an incomplete response. The complete response is: cry, stand, and try again.

What changes after the First Challenge is not status but access. A child who has passed is permitted in the forge. Permitted at the adult table during feast nights. Permitted to hold the family's soul-bars during the Reckoning Fire. Permitted to hear the full stories — the ones that aren't funny, the ones the adults tell after the children are supposed to be asleep. The First Challenge is not the moment a child becomes an adult. It is the moment the adults decide the child is strong enough to carry what the adults carry. The weight, as always with the Gorundai, is literal and metaphorical and the same thing.

---


---

### Thalessi (High Elves) — 10% of world population

**Physiology:** Tall, lean, angular features, tapered ears. Skin from pale ivory to warm bronze. Lifespan: 300-400 years. Physical decline only in the final two decades.
**Distribution:** Primarily Orenthas. Heartland: **Vethara**, semi-autonomous city-state predating the Masqarat by 800 years. Rare on Caldruun, almost absent from the Sundren.
**Dominion Tendency:** Exceptional Vision. Longevity compounds mastery — a 300-year-old Thalessi with Vision has had ten lifetimes of refinement.

**Cultural Role:** Archivists, diplomats, long-term strategists. A Thalessi politician thinks in centuries. They helped build the Masqarat, benefit from the system, and a growing number suspect it's rotting from the head.

**Naming:** Flowing syllables, soft consonants. Family names carry weight (Solavane, Kolmas-Verr, Sethmura). Children receive their first name at birth, family name at a private **Naming Ceremony** at age fifteen. Sharing the Naming Ceremony story with a non-Thalessi signals deep trust.

**Food:** Preservation and presentation. **Vetha-cured fish** (marinated three weeks), **spire bread** (two-day layered pastry), **archive tea** (seven herbs, each steeped at a different temperature — recipe varies by family, considered semi-confidential).

**Key Holidays:** **The Solace Vigil** (1 night, winter solstice) — all light extinguished at dusk. One hour of silence. Eldest lights a candle: "What was lost is remembered. What is remembered is not lost." Vethara opens the Solace Archive's public gallery for one night. Lines form at dawn. **The Accord Bridge** (3 days, midsummer) — Thalessi-Morventhi cooperation treaty observance. Gift exchange, formal dinner, joint concert. Always technically proficient and emotionally strained.

**Death Customs:** Three-day display in the family home. Cremation. Ashes in ceramic vessels inscribed with full name, family line, one chosen sentence. Interred in family archive vaults extending three stories underground.

**Marriage:** A **binding of archives** — the two families merge their private records. Literal. Divorce requires unbinding archives, which is logistically nightmarish.

*"They see everything, say little, and were already old when your grandmother was born."*

---


#### Humanization — Thalessi: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Thalessi**

A Thalessi archivist in Vethara wakes with the light. Not an alarm — the Thalessi do not use alarm devices, considering them an admission that one's internal discipline has failed. She has been waking at the same hour for ninety-three years and her body does not require instruction. Breakfast is spire bread — day-old, because fresh spire bread is a two-day production and only made for occasions — sliced thin, spread with pressed nut paste, eaten at a table set for one with a cloth napkin that she launders every third day regardless of whether it appears dirty. Archive tea steeps on the counter in a ceramic pot that belonged to her grandmother's grandmother. Seven herbs. The steeping temperatures are written nowhere. She learned them by watching her mother's hands at the kettle, the way her mother learned from hers. If you asked her to write the recipe down she could, but she would consider it a small betrayal — the tea is transmitted by presence, not by text.

The walk to the archive takes twelve minutes through Vethara's white stone streets, which are quiet at this hour because the Thalessi do not bustle. They walk at a pace that suggests they have somewhere to be but will not be diminished by hurrying to reach it. She passes the bakery where Tessavenne has been making spire bread for a hundred and forty years and has not once altered the recipe. She passes the memorial wall where the names of the Solace Vigil dead are carved small and close, so many centuries of names that the oldest have worn smooth and can only be read by running a fingertip along the grooves. She does this every morning with one name. She has not told anyone whose name it is.

At the archive she hangs her coat on the same hook — third from the door, left side — and begins the work she has done since she was forty-one: cataloguing, cross-referencing, annotating the vast accumulation of Thalessi record that is both Vethara's greatest treasure and its heaviest burden. She will eat a midday meal of vetha-cured fish and bitter greens at her desk, speaking to three or four colleagues in conversations that an outsider would find indistinguishable from formal negotiations. She will leave at dusk, walk home along the same route, steep the same tea, and read until the candle burns to the mark she set. The mark has not changed in decades. The books have. That is enough variety. The canal-water outside her window makes a sound against the stone embankment at night — a lapping rhythm governed by Aelund's pull — and she falls asleep to it the way she has for ninety-three years, and the sameness of it is not stagnation. It is the sound of a life that has chosen its shape and will not be moved from it.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Thalessi**

The Thalessi believe they are the world's great preservers. Their archives stretch three stories underground. Their family records span millennia. Their Solace Vigil exists to ensure that what is lost is remembered. Memory is sacred. Continuity is virtue. A Thalessi who allows knowledge to be destroyed has committed the only sin that Thalessi culture unanimously recognizes.

And yet the Thalessi helped build the Masqarat. They helped design its legal architecture, its institutional framework, its bureaucratic systems — the same systems that fund the Erasure Clerks who burn Second Age records for a living. This is not ancient history. It is current policy. The Masqarat's suppression apparatus runs on administrative structures that Thalessi minds designed, and Vethara's semi-autonomous status — its special legal code, its educational independence, its cultural protections — was purchased with that cooperation. The price of being allowed to preserve their own history was helping to destroy everyone else's.

Most Thalessi do not think about this. The ones who do think about it fall into two camps: those who argue that Vethara's autonomy justifies the compromise (preserving something is better than preserving nothing), and those who stare at the Solace Vigil's memorial wall and wonder how many names on it belonged to people whose records they helped erase. The second camp is growing. The first camp is louder. Neither camp has produced a Thalessi willing to actually break the arrangement, because breaking it would mean losing the archives — and a Thalessi who risks the archives risks everything the culture exists to protect. The trap is perfect. It was, arguably, designed by Thalessi minds. Which makes it worse.

The smaller contradiction is personal. Thalessi prize control — emotional, intellectual, physical. Composure is identity. A Thalessi who loses their temper in public has not merely made a mistake; they have revealed that the architecture of their personality has a flaw. And yet Thalessi art — their music, their poetry, the echo of what their culture produces when it stops performing competence — is saturated with longing. The Solace Vigil's central phrase is about loss. The archive tea recipes guarded like state secrets are, at root, recipes for comfort. The careful, calibrated exterior exists because the interior is not careful at all, and every Thalessi knows this about themselves and about each other, and no one says it aloud because saying it aloud would be losing control, and losing control is the one thing they cannot afford. So they write it into songs that run for twenty minutes and contain everything they will not say in conversation, and they call it art, and that is the compromise.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Thalessi**

What Thalessi say about other races during private dinners in Vethara's upper districts, after the second pot of archive tea, when the formality has thinned enough to show the edges:

Humans are brief. Not unintelligent — brief. A human lives eighty years and spends forty of them figuring out what the Thalessi knew at thirty. The Thalessi respect human energy — that frantic, compressed drive to accomplish everything before the clock runs out — but they find it exhausting to be near. Working with a human delegation means compressing a century of context into a three-day briefing and watching them make decisions based on a fraction of the picture. The Thalessi smile and accommodate. Privately: "They're making policy based on what happened last Tuesday. We're making policy based on what happened last century. And they wonder why we seem slow."

Gorundai are admired from a safe distance. Their smithwork is the best in the world — the Thalessi acknowledge this without reservation, which is rare — but their culture is an assault on Thalessi sensibilities. The directness. The volume. The idea that a dispute is best resolved by two people hitting each other behind a shed. A Thalessi who has spent three months carefully navigating a trade negotiation will watch a Gorundai delegation arrive, state their terms in four sentences, and leave, and the Thalessi will feel a complicated mixture of horror and envy that they will never, ever admit to.

Morventhi are the wound. Same ancestors. Same biology. Same ears, same lifespan, same capacity for Vision. The Morventhi chose to go underground and build a culture on honesty about desire. The Thalessi chose to stay above and build a culture on discipline and preservation. Every interaction between the two races carries three thousand years of "you chose wrong" flowing in both directions. The Accord Bridge holiday — three days of formal cooperation — is the annual performance of a relationship that has never healed and may never heal. The gifts are always appropriate. The dinner is always strained. The joint concert is always technically flawless and emotionally hollow. Thalessi who have Morventhi friends (they exist, increasingly, among younger generations) describe the friendship as "like arguing with a mirror that made different choices."

Khari are appreciated in the abstract and slightly patronized in practice. The Thalessi admire Khari oral tradition — it is, after the Thalessi archives themselves, the most reliable knowledge-preservation system in the world. But the admiration has a ceiling. A Thalessi scholar will cite a Khari warren-song as a source and then spend three paragraphs explaining why the oral format is less reliable than the written record, which the Khari consider insulting and the Thalessi consider accurate. The friction is structural: the Thalessi believe that knowledge lives in archives. The Khari believe that knowledge lives in people. Both are right. Neither can see the other's point without feeling that their own is diminished.

Rathai are invisible. Not deliberately — the Thalessi simply do not think about the Rathai with the same frequency they think about the other long-lived or culturally prominent races. A Thalessi in Vethara might go years without encountering a Rathai in a professional context. When forced to consider them, the Thalessi response is guilt-tinged and vague: "They build so much and receive so little." This is sincere and also useless. The Rathai in the Nettleworks do not need Thalessi sympathy. They need Thalessi political advocacy. The advocacy does not come because it would require the Thalessi to challenge the Masqarat's economic structure, and challenging the Masqarat risks Vethara's autonomy. The circle closes. The Rathai remain invisible.

Varrek are intellectual equals, which the Thalessi find both refreshing and threatening. A Varrek engineer who can match a Thalessi scholar's depth of knowledge in a different domain is a welcome dinner guest. A Varrek who can match them in their OWN domain is an existential challenge to the Thalessi self-concept. The Collegium Verossian in Tourrenne — technically a joint institution — has produced exactly three Varrek department heads in four hundred years. All three were controversially appointed. The Thalessi insist this is meritocratic. The Varrek keep count.

Oni are baffling. The volume, the festivals, the Challenge Toast, the entire cultural apparatus of a people who resolve romantic interest through dares — a Thalessi cannot process it. A Thalessi who attends an Oni Grand Festival comes back with the expression of someone who has visited another planet. "They set drinks on fire and then DRANK them." Secretly, some Thalessi envy the freedom. Most would die before admitting it.

Lunarians make the Thalessi uncomfortable in a way the Thalessi cannot quite name. The glow. The knowledge. The sense that the Lunarians remember things the Thalessi were supposed to preserve and didn't. A Thalessi archivist who meets a Lunarian memory-keeper feels, for a brief and terrible moment, like a fraud — because the Thalessi built an institution to do what the Lunarians do naturally, and the institution is larger, better funded, more impressive, and somehow less complete.

What other races say about the Thalessi: "They think they're better than you." This is the universal first impression, and it is both accurate and misleading. The Thalessi do not think they are better. They think they are more careful. They think they have had longer to consider the question and have therefore considered it more thoroughly. The distinction matters enormously to the Thalessi and not at all to anyone else, which is itself a very Thalessi problem.

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Thalessi**

A Thalessi in love is a Thalessi in trouble, and they know it.

The culture prizes composure. Romantic attraction is the single most reliable destroyer of composure in existence. A Thalessi who is falling for someone will, in the early stages, become more controlled — speaking more precisely, choosing words with even greater care, maintaining eye contact for exactly the correct duration. To an outsider, they appear unaffected. To another Thalessi, the overcorrection is a billboard. When a Thalessi starts speaking in perfectly structured sentences during casual conversation, their friends exchange glances and start quietly taking bets.

The first signal a Thalessi offers is intellectual. They will find reasons to debate the person they're interested in — not to win, but to extend the conversation. A Thalessi who argues with you past the point of necessity is not being difficult. They are trying to keep you in the room. The second signal is the tea. Offering to prepare archive tea for someone is, in Thalessi culture, an unmistakable declaration of romantic interest. The recipe is family knowledge. Sharing it — the specific temperatures, the specific timing, the herbs your grandmother chose — is sharing something that costs to give. A Thalessi who makes you tea and watches you drink it is watching to see if you understand what they've offered. If you do, the courtship has begun. If you don't, they will never mention it, and the hurt will live in them for decades because the Thalessi do not release things easily, including grief over what never happened.

The courtship itself is slow and layered. Three to six months of shared meals, shared work, shared silences that a Gorundai would find unbearable and a Thalessi finds necessary. The physical progression follows the emotional one — hand touches that linger a fraction longer than custom, a shoulder leaned against during a concert, the first kiss arriving after weeks of proximity like a sentence that has been carefully composed and is finally, finally spoken. A Thalessi first kiss is not spontaneous. It is the culmination of a sustained act of will, and it means that every defense the Thalessi maintains against the world has been, for this one person, deliberately lowered. The vulnerability of that gesture, in a culture built on control, cannot be overstated.

The deepest intimacy is not physical. It is the sharing of the Naming Ceremony story — the private family ritual at fifteen that gives a Thalessi their full identity. Some Thalessi have sex with dozens of partners across three centuries without ever sharing their Naming story. The body is offered before the history. When the history is finally offered, it means the Thalessi has decided that this person is not a season but a structure — something they are building their life around, something that will outlast convenience and comfort.

Thalessi grief is architectural in a different way than Gorundai grief. Where the Gorundai build outward — noise, fire, iron — the Thalessi build inward.

The first response to loss is stillness. A grieving Thalessi stops. Not dramatically — they simply become quieter than their already quiet baseline, and the people who know them recognize the difference the way you recognize a clock that has stopped. The house goes silent. The archive tea goes unmade. The routine that has sustained them for decades suspends, and in the gap, the loss fills every space the routine used to occupy.

The three-day display is formal — the body in the home, visitors received, condolences offered in prescribed phrases that have not changed in a thousand years. The Thalessi find comfort in the prescription. Grief is chaos. The ritual is structure. A Thalessi who can recite the correct response to each condolence is not being cold. They are holding the wall up with their hands while the foundation crumbles beneath it.

The ceramic vessel receives the ashes. The chosen sentence — one sentence, selected by the family, inscribed on the vessel — is the source of more agonized deliberation than any Thalessi will admit. How do you compress a three-hundred-year life into one sentence? You can't. The attempt is the point. Families have argued over the sentence for weeks. The sentence that is finally chosen is always inadequate and always the truest thing they could find.

A year later, two years, ten years — the Thalessi do not stop grieving. They integrate it. The loss becomes a room in the architecture of their interior life, a room they visit at specific times and leave at specific times, and this is not suppression. It is management. A Thalessi who has lost someone they loved carries the loss the way Vethara carries its archives — underground, organized, preserved, and heavy enough to shape the structure built above it. They do not speak of it often. When they do, the precision of the language is the tell. A Thalessi describing a loss in perfectly chosen words is not being clinical. They are being careful, because the alternative is being consumed, and being consumed means losing control, and losing control means the architecture fails, and if the architecture fails there is nothing between them and the weight of three hundred years of accumulated feeling, and that weight would flatten them.

So they choose their words. And the words hold the wall. And the wall holds.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Thalessi**

Thalessi children are patient by the standards of other races and chaotic by the standards of their own. A Thalessi seven-year-old can sit through a two-hour lesson on family history without complaint — an accomplishment that would be impressive in a human child twice the age — and then spend the next three hours disassembling a mechanical clock to see how it works, failing to reassemble it, hiding the pieces, and maintaining a calm, blank expression when questioned. Thalessi children do not throw tantrums. They scheme. A Thalessi toddler who wants something forbidden will not cry for it. They will wait, watch the pattern of adult attention, and act during the gap. Thalessi parents describe the first twenty years of child-rearing as "being outwitted by someone who can't yet tie their shoes."

The games are quiet and competitive. **Vetha-stones** — a placement game played with polished river stones on a gridded board — is the standard childhood obsession. Rules are simple. Strategy is infinite. Thalessi children begin playing at five and by twelve have developed personal styles that their parents analyze with the same seriousness a Gorundai parent gives to their child's thrakkha performance. The difference is visibility: a Gorundai child's prowess is physical and public. A Thalessi child's prowess is mental and private. A child who wins a vetha-stones tournament in Vethara's youth league receives quiet family pride. A child who loses receives quiet family coaching. In neither case does anyone raise their voice.

What they get in trouble for: impatience. Not impoliteness — a Thalessi child who is rude to an elder will be corrected so swiftly and thoroughly that it rarely happens twice. But a child who interrupts, who acts before thinking, who blurts the answer instead of waiting to be asked — this is the recurring Thalessi parental struggle. The phrase every Thalessi child hears: **"Veth seluura, kenn ossal"** — roughly, "The thought precedes the mouth." Meaning: you knew the answer but you did not wait, and the not-waiting is the failure, not the knowing. Every adult Thalessi can recite this. Every adult Thalessi also remembers being thirteen and wanting to scream because they DID think first and the adult just didn't see it, and the injustice of being told to be patient by someone who has had two hundred years of practice at it is the central grievance of every Thalessi adolescence.

The Naming Ceremony at fifteen is the rite of passage. The details are family-private — no two families conduct it identically, and the specific form is part of what makes sharing the story so intimate later in life. What is known: the child receives their family name in a ceremony attended only by blood relatives. Something is said. Something is given. The child who walks into the ceremony is not the same person who walks out, not because of transformation but because of knowledge — they now carry the family's private history, the version that isn't in the public archive, the version that includes the failures and the shames and the decisions the family made that the archive records have been carefully edited to obscure. A fifteen-year-old Thalessi after their Naming Ceremony is quieter than they were before. Not sad. Heavier. They have been told what they come from, and the weight of it is the first adult thing they carry.

What changes after the Naming is not permission — Thalessi children are permitted most things from a young age, because the culture trusts in early competence. What changes is expectation. Before the Naming, mistakes are learning. After the Naming, mistakes are choices. The distinction is the sharpest line in Thalessi childhood, and every Thalessi remembers the first time they erred after their Naming and saw, in a parent's expression, not disappointment at a child's failure but assessment of an adult's judgment. That look — measuring, not forgiving — is the moment childhood ends. The Thalessi do not mark it with ceremony. The Naming already happened. The look is just the confirmation that it took.

---

### Morventhi (Dark Elves) — 8% of world population

**Physiology:** Same base as Thalessi; three thousand years of separate development produced differences. Skin trends darker (deep brown to blue-black), eyes adapted for low-light with reflective tapeta. Same 300-400 year lifespan.
**Distribution:** Heartland: the **Kethrun Undersprawl**, seventeen-level underground city beneath central Caldruun. Surface communities across Caldruun and in Orenthan port cities.
**Dominion Tendency:** Balanced Vision and Armoring. Combat doctrine treats dual-expression fighting as baseline.

**Cultural Role:** Split from the Thalessi ~3,000 years ago over Second Age guilt. Meritocratic to a fault — status determined by accomplishment. This produces brilliant individuals and a society where trust is perpetually conditional.

**Naming:** Harder consonants than Thalessi — guttural stops, doubled consonants, shorter syllables. Family names hyphenated with Undersprawl level of origin (Kethvaine, Malcross). Dropping the level-name signals exile or deliberate separation.

**Food:** What grows in the dark. **Kethroot** (starchy tuber, geothermal-heated soil), dozens of mushroom varieties, cave-bred goat. Signature dish: **deep-char** — kethroot sliced thin, charred on vent-stone, served with mushroom sauce (fermented six months on Level 7, raw on Level 4 — the disagreement is violent).

**Art:** Architecture (the Undersprawl itself is sculpture, every beam inscribed with Vasuur glyphs) and **echo-singing** — single voices producing harmonics that fill cathedral-sized caverns. Approximately forty active professionals. Two decades of training.

**Key Holidays:** **The Descent** (1 day, tied to geothermal vent-spikes) — work stops. All Morventhi travel as deep as they can reach. Hand on stone wall, one minute of silence. Then feast. **The Quieting** (1 evening, midwinter) — every echo-singer performs simultaneously from different levels. Dissonant, overlapping waves filling the Undersprawl for forty minutes. The silence afterward is heavier than the singing.

**Coming-of-Age:** **The Mapping** at age forty — solo journey through three Undersprawl levels without guidance. Map what you find. Journal kept for life, never shared.

**Death Customs:** Bodies placed in carved niches in Undersprawl walls — thousands of niches line every corridor, each sealed with flat stone inscribed with name, birth level, and a family-chosen Vasuur glyph.

**Marriage:** **Joining of Levels** — the couple chooses which level to live on. Politically loaded.

*"They'll smile at you, outperform you, and never once explain how."*

---


#### Humanization — Morventhi: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Morventhi**

A Morventhi glassblower on Level 7 of the Kethrun Undersprawl wakes to the pulse of the vent-cycle — a low, rhythmic surge of geothermal pressure through the stone that the Undersprawl's residents feel in their teeth before they hear it with their ears. There is no dawn underground. The day begins when the bioluminescent fungi along the corridor ceiling shift from their dim night-register to full output, a slow brightening that takes twenty minutes and gives the entire level the quality of a permanent, gentle dusk. He eats kethroot fried in cave-goat fat — yesterday's leftovers, reheated on a vent-stone shelf that every Morventhi kitchen is built around, a flat basalt slab positioned over a minor thermal fissure that stays at cooking temperature year-round. The shelf is the center of the home. Everything else — sleeping quarters, storage, the basin for washing — arranges itself around the warm stone.

He walks to his workshop through corridors that are never empty. Level 7 holds four hundred thousand people in a space carved from living rock, and the corridors are the Undersprawl's streets — twelve feet wide, arched, every support beam inscribed with Vasuur glyphs that his grandmother's generation carved and his generation maintains with annual retracing. The foot traffic is constant. A Morventhi navigates their home level the way a fish navigates a reef — by feel, by memory, by the specific echoes that each junction produces. He knows he's passed the Level 7 baths by the sound of water and conversation spilling through the entry arch. He knows he's approaching the workshop district by the shift in air temperature — warmer, drier, the smell of silica dust and hot metal.

The workshop is shared with two other glassblowers. They nod. The work begins. Morventhi glasswork is a minor art — not as celebrated as echo-singing, not as essential as smithwork — but the Undersprawl's bioluminescent lighting creates a market for glass that captures and refracts the blue-green fungal glow, and his pieces sell steadily at the Crucible Market on Level 4. The midday meal is taken at the workshop: deep-char from a vendor who walks the district with a heated cart, mushroom sauce on the side, the Level 7 fermented variety because the Level 4 raw preparation is, as far as he is concerned, an insult to the concept of food. This opinion is not negotiable. He has ended two friendships over it. He considers both endings justified.

Afternoons he works. Evenings he bathes — the Level 7 communal baths, geothermally heated, carved into a natural cavern that holds sixty. He has gone to the same bath at the same hour for thirty years. The conversations there are the spine of his social life: who's producing what, whose echo-singing recital is worth attending, which Level representative said something foolish in the Assembly, and whether the new kethroot harvest from the Level 12 farms is as good as last season's (it never is, according to everyone over fifty, regardless of the actual quality). He eats dinner at home — cave-goat stew, more kethroot, a glass of Level 11 mineral wine that tastes of iron and earth and costs more than it should. He falls asleep to the vibration of the Undersprawl itself — seventeen levels of carved stone humming with geothermal energy, water moving through channels, a million and a half people breathing in the dark. The sound is not silence. It is a city dreaming in stone.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Morventhi**

The Morventhi believe they left the Thalessi behind because the Thalessi chose lies. The foundational story — the Vetha-moren descending underground three thousand years ago — is a story about rejecting the surface world's complicity in the Second Age suppression. The Morventhi went down because the truth mattered more than comfort. They built a culture on honesty — honest desire, honest ambition, honest assessment of merit. A Morventhi who hides what they want is violating the principle the civilization was founded on. Openness is not just valued. It is constitutional.

And yet the Deep Archive on Level 14 contains a sealed section that no civilian Morventhi has ever entered. The Silverborn-script documents found near the Third Eddan Tree roots — the very truth that the Morventhi supposedly descended to protect — sit behind locked doors, guarded by archivists who have never read them, preserved for millennia by a culture that prizes openness above all things. The Morventhi left the surface because the surface suppressed knowledge. The Morventhi then sealed knowledge of their own. The justification is preservation — the documents are fragile, the script is dangerous, the Masqarat would destroy them if they knew the full extent. All of this may be true. None of it resolves the contradiction. A culture built on honesty has a locked room at its center, and the lock is old enough that most Morventhi have stopped noticing it's there.

The second contradiction is meritocratic. Morventhi culture determines status by accomplishment — what you have done, not who your family is. This is genuine. A Morventhi from Level 17 who produces brilliant work will rise to recognition regardless of origin. But Level 7 — the residential and political heart — concentrates wealth, education, and social networks in a way that makes accomplishment significantly easier for those born into it. A Level 17 child and a Level 7 child are theoretically equal. In practice, the Level 7 child has access to better workshops, better teachers, and a social network that amplifies their early work. The meritocracy is real. The starting conditions are not equal. The Morventhi acknowledge this when pressed and then change the subject, because the alternative is admitting that their system produces the same stratification as the surface world's, just with different names for the tiers.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Morventhi**

What Morventhi say about other races in the communal baths, where the acoustics carry every opinion and nobody pretends otherwise:

Thalessi are the Morventhi's oldest argument. Same blood, different choices. A Morventhi who meets a Thalessi sees, in their face, the person they might have been if their ancestors had chosen daylight over truth. This makes every interaction personal in a way that interactions with other races are not. The Morventhi think the Thalessi are cowards — brilliant, beautiful, three-hundred-year-old cowards who sold their integrity for archive vaults and semi-autonomous status. The Thalessi think the Morventhi are addicts — pleasure-obsessed cave-dwellers who confused hedonism with honesty. Both accusations are unfair. Both contain enough truth to sting. The Accord Bridge holiday exists to demonstrate that the two cultures can cooperate. It demonstrates, every year, that they can cooperate for exactly three days before someone brings up the split and the evening ends in diplomatic ice.

Humans are tolerated with a patience that borders on condescension. The Morventhi have been building underground cities for three thousand years. Humans have been building above-ground cities for roughly the same duration and somehow still haven't solved drainage. A Morventhi engineer visiting Thalveris for the first time will spend an hour examining the sewer system and come away personally offended. "They have Varrek helping them and it STILL doesn't work properly." The Morventhi respect human adaptability — the ability to thrive in any environment is genuinely admired — but find human emotional opacity maddening. A Morventhi who can't tell what you're feeling assumes you're hiding something. Humans are always hiding something, even when they're not, because their faces don't communicate at the resolution the Morventhi expect.

Gorundai are liked. Straightforward, the Morventhi say. The compliment is genuine — in a world of hedging and diplomacy, the Gorundai commitment to direct speech mirrors the Morventhi's own, just louder and with more physical contact. The friction is about aesthetics. Morventhi craft — echo-singing, glasswork, the carved beauty of the Undersprawl's architecture — is precise, refined, layered. Gorundai craft — forge-work, soul-bars, the raw iron of their traditions — is functional, heavy, blunt. A Morventhi glassblower who visits a Gorundai forge will admire the heat and the skill and then wince at the lack of finish. "They make the strongest blades in the world and then don't polish them." The Gorundai consider polishing a blade vanity. The Morventhi consider it respect for the material. The argument is aesthetic, ancient, and unresolvable.

Rathai are understood. The Morventhi and the Rathai both know what it means to build a civilization in a space that wasn't designed for it — the Undersprawl carved from stone, the Nettleworks crammed into urban margins. There is a solidarity between the two races that transcends cultural difference. A Rathai engineer working on Level 11 is treated as a colleague, not a guest. The Morventhi also pay better than the surface employers, and the Rathai know it, and this economic reality has produced the only significant non-Morventhi population in the Undersprawl's working levels.

Khari are admired for their freedom and pitied for their lifespan. A Morventhi who lives three hundred years watching a Khari friend age and die in sixty feels the disparity like a slow wound. Cross-racial friendships between Morventhi and Khari are common in port cities and consistently painful for the Morventhi half. "You watch them burn so brightly and then they're gone and you're still HERE." This grief is specific to long-lived races paired with short-lived ones, and the Morventhi carry it openly because their culture does not permit hiding pain.

Varrek are the Morventhi's second-closest professional allies after the Rathai. The Undersprawl's infrastructure — ventilation, water management, structural engineering — relies on Varrek principles adapted by Morventhi builders. The respect is mutual and deep. The friction is philosophical: the Varrek believe knowledge should be free. The Morventhi believe knowledge should be honest. These are not the same thing. A Varrek who publishes a paper that is technically accurate but contextually misleading has satisfied Varrek ethics and violated Morventhi ones. The argument surfaces every time a joint research project reaches the publication stage.

Oni and Lunarians occupy different positions in the Morventhi imagination. Oni are fun — a Morventhi who has attended a Grand Festival comes home converted to the idea that the surface world has at least one culture worth visiting. Lunarians are sacred. The Morventhi remember, in the way that long-lived races remember, that the Silverborn were real. The Lunarians are what remains. A Morventhi who meets a Lunarian treats them with a reverence that makes the Lunarian uncomfortable, because the Morventhi are seeing the ancestors and the Lunarian is just trying to get through the conversation.

What other races say about the Morventhi: "They never come up for air." Both literally and figuratively. The surface perception of the Morventhi is a people so committed to their underground world that they've become insular, self-referencing, and slightly alien. A surface-dweller visiting the Undersprawl for the first time finds the communal bathing unsettling, the social openness aggressive, and the darkness oppressive. The Morventhi find the surface-dweller's discomfort amusing and slightly offensive. "You think we're strange because we're honest. What does that say about you?"

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Morventhi**

A Morventhi in love does not hide it. Cannot hide it. The culture was built on the principle that desire acknowledged is desire respected, and a Morventhi who feels attraction signals it through proximity, through touch, through direct statement. There is no Morventhi equivalent of the Thalessi's careful overcorrection or the Gorundai's thrakkenvoss posturing. A Morventhi who wants someone says "I want you" and the sentence is not crude — it is the culture's highest form of respect. You are worth honesty.

What makes Morventhi love complicated is not the beginning but the middle. The courtship is fast. The physical intimacy follows quickly — the Descent baths, the warmrooms, the cultural framework that treats sex as connection rather than culmination. A Morventhi couple might sleep together within weeks of meeting. The question that takes years is whether the connection means more than connection. The Morventhi distinguish sharply between **vethashel** — a pleasant evening, a warm night, a body beside yours that you enjoyed and may never see again — and the slow, accumulating weight of a partnership that becomes structural. A Morventhi who has had a hundred vethashel encounters and one real partnership will tell you the hundred were beautiful and the one was terrifying, because the one required something the hundred didn't: the willingness to be known completely by someone who could hurt you with what they know.

The Joining of Levels — the marriage ceremony — is public, political, and celebratory. The Deep Night that follows it is the only sexual act the Morventhi consider private. The contrast is deliberate. A culture that has made openness its foundation marks the deepest commitment by closing the door. What happens during the Deep Night is never discussed. Not with friends, not with family, not with future partners if the marriage ends. The silence around the Deep Night is the Morventhi's single concession to privacy, and its rarity makes it sacred. A Morventhi who violates the Deep Night's privacy has committed the culture's most intimate betrayal — not of the partner, but of the principle that some things become more meaningful when they are protected from the world's gaze.

Morventhi grief is loud and then it is not.

The first response is sound. Echo-singing tradition holds that the dead deserve a voice, and the family of the deceased will sing — not a formal performance but a raw, unstructured sound that fills the corridors of whatever level they're on. Neighbors hear it. The Undersprawl's acoustics carry it. On Level 7, where four hundred thousand people live in carved stone, a death-song can be heard three junctions away, and people who hear it stop, turn toward the sound, and wait. Not to join — the song belongs to the family. To witness. The Morventhi believe that grief unheard is grief dishonored.

The body goes to the wall. The niche is carved — sometimes pre-carved, sometimes done in the hours after death by a family member who needs the work of hands to survive the work of feeling. The flat stone seal is inscribed: name, birth level, Vasuur glyph chosen by the family. The choice of glyph is agonizing. Each glyph means something — stability, endurance, beauty, depth — and the family must compress the dead into a single symbol that will outlast every other record of their existence. Families have fought over the glyph choice. The fights are forgiven. The glyph, once carved, is permanent.

The weeks after are the hardest part, because the Morventhi do not have a ritual for ongoing grief. The death-song happens once. The niche is sealed once. After that, the culture's insistence on openness means that a grieving Morventhi is expected to say what they feel, and what they feel is often nothing they have words for. A Morventhi who sits in the baths and stares at the water without speaking is broadcasting a grief that every other bather recognizes and no one interrupts. Someone will bring food later. Someone will sit nearby without talking. The Morventhi do not have a word for this specific kindness — the presence without intrusion — because they do it so automatically that naming it would feel like naming breathing.

A year later, the Morventhi visits the niche. Touches the stone. Speaks to the dead if they need to, silently or aloud. The corridors of the Undersprawl are lined with thousands of sealed niches, and a walk through any residential level is a walk through centuries of death, and the Morventhi do not find this morbid. They find it honest. The dead are here. They have always been here. The living walk among them every day and the proximity is not a haunting — it is a conversation that never ends, conducted in stone, between the people who are gone and the people who continue.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Morventhi**

Morventhi children grow up in stone, in warmth, and in public. The Undersprawl does not permit the kind of isolation that surface cultures offer children — private rooms, separated spaces, the luxury of a yard. A Morventhi child's world is the corridor, the communal baths, the market, the workshop. Privacy is something they learn exists around the time they learn that the surface has a sky. Both concepts are equally abstract until experienced.

By five, a Morventhi child can navigate their home level alone. Not because their parents are negligent — because the Undersprawl's corridors are a community, and every adult in the corridor is, by Morventhi convention, an auxiliary parent. A child running unsupervised through Level 7 will be fed by the vendor at the junction, corrected by the glassblower whose workshop they're shouting past, and returned home by the bath attendant who recognizes whose child this is by the echo of their footsteps. The Morventhi say "the level raises the child," and they mean it literally. A parent who tries to raise a child in isolation is gently and then firmly redirected by the community, because isolation is the surface disease and the Undersprawl was built to cure it.

The games are acoustic. **Echorun** — a chase game played in the corridors where the child being chased makes no sound and the pursuers navigate by echoes alone — is the Morventhi equivalent of the Gorundai's thrakkha. It teaches the stone-sense that every Undersprawl resident carries: the ability to read a corridor's shape by its sound, to know the difference between a dead-end and a through-passage by the way a footstep reverberates. Children who are good at echorun are admired. Children who are exceptional at it are watched by the echo-singing academies, because the same acoustic sensitivity that makes a brilliant echorun player makes a potential performer.

What they get in trouble for: dishonesty. Not lying about large things — a child who steals is corrected by the community with a swiftness that makes repetition unlikely. The trouble is small dishonesty. Pretending not to feel something. Hiding a hurt to avoid appearing weak. Saying "I'm fine" when they're not. A Morventhi parent who catches their child suppressing an emotion will stop everything and address it with an intensity that surprises children of other races. **"Thress vetha-kenn"** — "speak the real thing" — is the phrase every Morventhi child hears, and it is not a suggestion. It is the culture's central demand, applied from the earliest age. A Morventhi child who learns to say what they actually feel, even when it's ugly, even when it's inconvenient, has passed the most important threshold. A child who learns to hide has failed the culture's most basic test.

The Mapping at forty is the formal rite of passage — solo journey through three Undersprawl levels without guidance, returning with a personal map and a journal. But forty is late by Morventhi standards. The real transition from child to adult happens earlier and less formally, somewhere between twenty and thirty, when a Morventhi young person produces their first work of genuine merit — a piece of craft, a performance, an administrative accomplishment, a feat of engineering — and the community responds not with encouragement but with evaluation. The shift from "well done for someone your age" to "this is good" is the line. No ceremony marks it. The Morventhi do not need a ceremony. They need the work. The work speaks, and the community listens, and if what it hears is real, the child is gone and the adult has arrived. The adult will remember the exact moment. The community will pretend they always knew.

---

### Khari (Beastkin) — 10% of world population

**Physiology:** Humanoid with pronounced animal traits — fur, tails, claws, ears. Expression varies by hereditary lineage: canine, feline, avian, cervine, and dozens of rarer lines. Enhanced senses (biological, not Dominion). Lifespan: 60-70 years.
**Distribution:** Nomadic. Extended family groups called **warrens** (30-200 individuals) following seasonal routes across both continents and the Merchant Spine. Several semi-permanent settlements in the Vushan Freeholds.
**Dominion Tendency:** Natural Vision affinity — enhanced senses provide a running start. Often develops spontaneously during adolescence.

**Cultural Role:** The world's connective tissue. Carry news, goods, oral histories between regions. Khari oral tradition is the oldest continuous knowledge system — their songs referenced the Sundren centuries before the Sovereign King confirmed it existed.

**Naming:** Lineage-coded — phonetic markers indicate animal lineage. Canine: hard plosives, short vowels (Teshken, Drukk). Feline: sibilants, longer vowels (Osseanne, Suusha). Avian: aspirated consonants, rising tones (Wisp — single syllable indicating self-chosen name). Cervine: nasal consonants, rounded vowels (Monneth, Nuura). Warren surnames shared by all members; leaving a warren and joining another means changing names.

**Lineage Customs:** Canine (~40% of Khari): pack-social, hierarchy by distance traveled, forehead-press greeting for scent-exchange. Feline (~25%): solitary-adjacent, decisions through body language, smaller warrens. Avian (~20%): height-oriented, rooftops and masts, vertical social positioning. Cervine (~15%): largest, most reserved, strictest seasonal migration, most resistant to permanent settlement.

**Named Warrens:**
- **Warren Kaelith** (canine, ~180 members, Kelvanni island) — navigational services, reef pilots. Maintains **path-song archive** — oral route recordings encoded in melodic system mapping terrain to musical intervals.
- **Warren Thessivaan** (feline, ~60, Scattermarks) — intelligence and counter-surveillance. Led by Vasselen Thessivaan, former Tidewatch informant.
- **Warren Oullgrove** (cervine, ~120, central Caldruun migration) — herbal medicine and seasonal agriculture.
- **Warren Suuvi** (avian, ~90, Merrenbach reef) — reef navigation and pearl harvesting.

**Food:** Migration food. **Trail-bread** — flat bread baked with local grain, folded around dried meat and berries. Keeps for weeks. "A full belly is a gift. Trail-bread is a plan."

**Key Holidays:** **The Path-Singing** (3 days, spring) — warrens share path-songs when migration routes intersect. Routes where Khari died are sung in minor key. Navigation IS prayer. **The Still-Night** (observed when a warren member dies) — the warren stops moving. Silence dusk to dawn. At dawn, the eldest speaks the dead person's name and their most-walked path. Then the warren moves again.

**Coming-of-Age:** The **First Walk** — solo journey to a destination chosen by the trail-leader. Canine: 200+ miles. Feline: shorter but hostile terrain. Avian: navigation to a specific height.

**Death Customs:** Buried wherever death occurred. Burial site added to warren path-song as permanent waypoint. Walking a Khari path-song is walking through a graveyard you can only hear.

**Marriage:** **Path-Binding** — the couple walks a new route together, and the route becomes their shared song. Private. If the marriage ends, the route is unsung.

*"If you need to know something, find a Khari. If you need to keep a secret, don't."*

---


#### Humanization — Khari: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Khari**

A Khari canine-lineage trail scout in a warren of sixty wakes because the pack wakes. Not simultaneously — the warren's sleeping arrangement is communal, bodies overlapping in a sprawl of fur and warmth, and the first riser's movement sends a ripple of half-waking through the pile that resolves, over twenty minutes, into a staggered emergence. Breakfast is whatever the overnight forager brought back — dried fish from the coast if the warren is near the Bastion Sea, smoked root vegetables if they're inland, and always, everywhere, **path-bread**: a flat, dense unleavened round baked on stones the night before, meant to be torn and shared, not sliced and portioned. A Khari who eats without tearing off a piece for the person beside them has committed an offense so basic that it doesn't have a name — it would be like naming the offense of forgetting to breathe.

She checks the warren's path-markers while the children eat. Path-markers are the Khari's navigation system — carved or painted symbols on trees, rocks, bridge posts, and occasionally buildings, indicating safe routes, water sources, danger zones, and territorial boundaries. The markers are lineage-specific: canine warrens use scent-based markers invisible to non-Khari; avian warrens use aerial markers placed at heights only flyers can check; feline warrens use scratch-marks in patterns that look random but encode direction. Maintaining the markers is the trail scout's primary job, and she walks the morning route the way a Morventhi walks their corridor — by feel, by memory, by the quality of air and sound that tells her what has changed since yesterday.

Midday she returns. The warren has moved half a mile — a short shift, following the grazing pattern of the herd they're trailing, or the market rotation of the coastal town they're trading with. Nothing about a Khari warren is fixed. The sleeping site from last night is abandoned by noon. The cooking stones are carried. The children walk or ride or are carried depending on age. The only permanent structure is the warren-chest — a locked wooden box containing the warren's genealogy, its path-song records, and whatever trade goods are too valuable to display. The chest is the warren's heart. It moves when they move. A warren that loses its chest has lost its identity.

Afternoon is work: trading, scouting, mending, negotiating passage through another warren's territory (a process conducted through the exchange of path-songs — a melodic sequence that encodes route information and doubles as a greeting, a credential, and a peace offering). Evening is the warren gathering — food shared, stories told, disputes aired. Khari dispute resolution is vocal and brief: both parties speak, the eldest listens, the eldest decides. Appeals are rare because the eldest's decision is backed by the entire warren's social pressure, and a Khari who defies the group does not survive as a Khari — they survive as a person without a warren, which is the same as a person without a world.

She falls asleep in the pile again. The sound is breathing — sixty bodies in various states of wakefulness, the rustling of children shifting, the low murmur of the night-watch pair who talk in whispers not because the dark demands it but because the warren is a family sleeping and you do not raise your voice in a family's bedroom. The stars are different tonight than they were last week because the warren has moved south. She notes this the way she notes everything: by feel, in the body, where the knowledge lives.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Khari**

The Khari say they belong to no one. No government, no fixed border, no permanent address. The warren is home. The path is home. The idea that a piece of land can be owned — that you can draw a line on a map and declare everything inside it yours — is, to the Khari, a form of madness that the settled races have agreed to share. The Khari walk where they walk. They have always walked where they walk. Their path-songs predate the Masqarat, predate the Masqarat's predecessor, predate the concept of the nation-state. The Khari do not recognize borders because borders are younger than their routes.

And yet the Khari are the most territorial people in the world. Warren territories are mapped, maintained, defended, and inherited with a ferocity that would make a Gorundai clan chief nod in recognition. A canine warren whose trail markers are overwritten by another warren will respond with a speed and violence that contradicts every claim of nomadic freedom. The Khari distinction — "we do not own the land, we own the path" — is philosophically elegant and practically identical to territorial control. The path goes where it goes. If your village is on the path, the Khari will walk through it whether you like it or not. If your road cuts across the path, the Khari will use the road and resent you for building it. The freedom they claim is real. The territory they deny is also real. Both exist simultaneously, and the Khari do not experience this as a contradiction because they have never accepted the framework in which it would be one.

The deeper contradiction is about memory. The Khari pride themselves on their oral tradition — the oldest continuous knowledge system in the world, songs that reference the Sundren centuries before anyone confirmed it existed. The Khari remember everything. Except the split. The 3,000-year division between Renn-descended warrens and Path-Hider warrens is the defining fracture in Khari society, and neither side will sing the other's version of why it happened. The Khari, who carry the world's most complete oral record, have a hole in their own history that they maintain deliberately, through silence, through the refusal to learn the other side's song. A people who remember everything have chosen to forget the one thing that would force them to confront whether their own community was wrong.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Khari**

What Khari say about other races around the warren fire, between songs, in the hour when the children are asleep and the adults are honest:

Humans are everywhere and nowhere. They fill every space, build on every surface, and a Khari who walks a route that hasn't been walked in three years will find a human settlement in the middle of it. The Khari do not hate humans for this — hatred would require taking it personally, and the Khari regard human expansion the way they regard weather. It happens. You adjust. You walk around. But there is a weariness in how the Khari discuss it. Every decade, there are more human buildings and fewer open paths. Every decade, the warren-routes require more negotiation, more detours, more of the path-song sung in a different key because the original route is now a road or a farm or a port. The Khari word for this — **rennavoss**, roughly "the shrinking" — is spoken without anger and with a grief that humans rarely notice because it doesn't look like grief. It looks like quiet.

Thalessi are respected and resented. Respected because they value knowledge as the Khari do. Resented because they value it differently. A Thalessi scholar who cites a Khari path-song in an academic paper will footnote it, source it, and then spend three paragraphs explaining its limitations as a historical record compared to written archives. The Khari do not object to being cited. They object to being footnoted. The knowledge is not an artifact to be catalogued. It is a living thing, carried in a living voice, and the Thalessi insistence on converting it to text is, from the Khari perspective, a form of taxidermy — you preserve the shape and kill the animal.

Gorundai are easy. The Khari and the Gorundai have traded for centuries, share similar values around directness, and find each other's company uncomplicated. The friction is minor: Gorundai think the Khari move too much. The Khari think the Gorundai are stuck. A Gorundai who says "I've lived in this house for forty years" means it as a statement of stability. A Khari hears it as a description of imprisonment. Neither side pushes the point because the trade is good and the relationship doesn't need to survive philosophical alignment.

Morventhi are the Khari's most surprising allies. Both cultures are built around knowledge carried in bodies rather than books — the Morventhi in echo-singing, the Khari in path-songs. A Khari who visits the Undersprawl recognizes the acoustic sensitivity immediately and feels, for the first time in a settled space, something approaching home. The warmth of the stone corridors. The communal sleeping isn't that far from the warren pile. The honesty isn't that far from the fire-circle's openness. Khari-Morventhi friendships are common and carry a specific tenderness: two peoples who live in different directions — one horizontal across the land, one vertical into the stone — but who listen to the world the same way.

Rathai are kindred in a different register. The Nettleworks and the warren are both systems of survival in hostile space — one urban, one nomadic — and the Khari and the Rathai recognize each other's resourcefulness. Cross-race pairings between Khari and Rathai are common in port cities, producing "half-paw" children who are welcomed in warrens without hesitation. The Khari do not discriminate against mixed children. A child is a child. A child in the warren is the warren's child. This principle is non-negotiable and is, quietly, the thing the Khari are most proud of about themselves.

Varrek are puzzling. The Khari navigate by song, by instinct, by a knowledge that lives in the feet and the ears. The Varrek navigate by instrument, by calculation, by a knowledge that lives in numbers. When a Khari path-singer and a Varrek cartographer produce maps of the same route, the maps are equally accurate and completely different in form. The Khari find this amusing. The Varrek find it distressing. "How can you know where you are without measuring?" "How can you know where you are if you're staring at the measurement instead of the path?"

Oni are enjoyed. The Khari and the Oni share a love of noise, of gathering, of the specific energy that fills a space when too many people are having too good a time. A Khari warren that passes through the Sennaku Highlands during Grand Festival season will stay for the duration, and the resulting cross-cultural celebration is one of the few events in the world where two entirely different peoples are, for a week, indistinguishable in their enthusiasm.

Lunarians are sacred. The Khari oral tradition remembers the Silverborn. The Lunarians are the Silverborn's descendants. A Khari who meets a Lunarian will offer a path-song unprompted — a gift of knowledge given freely, without trade, without expectation. This is the highest honor a Khari can offer a stranger. The Lunarians usually don't know what to do with it.

What other races say about the Khari: "You can't pin them down." Meant as criticism — they don't settle, don't invest, don't commit to institutions. The Khari hear it as a compliment. You can't pin down the wind either. The wind was here before the pin.

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Khari**

How a Khari falls in love depends on the lineage, and the lineage differences are real enough that cross-lineage romances within the Khari are as culturally complex as cross-racial romances outside them.

A canine Khari in love becomes more present. The pack-social baseline is already physical — shared sleeping, casual grooming, the constant proximity of bodies — so the shift from friendship to desire is subtle. The canine Khari who's falling for someone will groom them longer than necessary, sleep closer in the pile, and find reasons to be on the same task rotation. Their friends notice within days. The object of interest notices within weeks. The warren notices within hours. A canine warren is a gossip engine powered by proximity and scent, and the lovestruck canine who thinks they're being subtle is broadcasting at a frequency every nose in the warren can read. When the approach comes, it's warm, physical, and embedded in the pack's daily life — not a dramatic declaration but a shift in the sleeping arrangement that everyone pretends to be surprised by and no one is.

A feline Khari in love becomes invisible. The solitary instinct inverts: instead of orbiting at a distance, the feline draws closer — but only when the other person isn't looking. Gifts appear. A repaired tool, a fresh kill left where they'll find it, a path-marker re-carved with unusual care on their preferred route. The feline Khari in love is the world's most attentive ghost, and the person being courted often doesn't realize it's happening until they notice the pattern — the accumulation of small, anonymous kindnesses that, when mapped, all point to the same source. When the feline finally closes the distance, the intensity is startling. All the attention that was distributed secretly is now focused directly, and the experience of being the full subject of a feline Khari's focus is, as other lineages describe it, "like being the only star in the sky."

An avian Khari in love flies. Literally. The courtship display is aerial, public, and gorgeous — patterns traced against the sky, altitude contests, the specific wing-spread posture that is biologically unmistakable. The paradox: the courtship is the most public display in Khari culture, and the consummation is the most private. An avian pair will retreat to the highest accessible point and will not be seen again until they choose to descend. Interrupting is a violation severe enough to cause warren-level consequences. The sky is for showing. The height is for keeping.

Khari grief is fast and total and then it moves.

A Khari who loses someone — a partner, a parent, a child, a warren-mate — will keen. The sound is animal and specific to the Khari: a high, carrying note that doesn't modulate, that holds in the air like a wire pulled taut, and that can be heard, in open terrain, for a mile. The warren hears it and stops. Everything stops. The children are gathered. The fires are banked. The warren contracts around the grieving member like a fist closing, bodies pressing close, the physical density of the pack saying what words cannot: you are not alone in this. We are the wall. The wall holds.

The body is returned to the path. Khari burial is exposure — the body placed in open ground along the warren's route, left for the wind and the animals and the weather. This horrifies settled races. The Khari do not bury and do not burn because the Khari do not stay. To bury is to fix the dead in a place the living will leave. To burn is to destroy what the world should reclaim. The body goes back to the ground it walked on. The path-song adds a verse. The warren moves on.

The moving on is the grief. A Khari warren that has lost a member does not stop traveling. The route continues. The path-song is sung with the new verse, and the new verse is the dead person's name and the place they were left, and every time the warren passes that point on the route — next season, and the season after, and the season after that — the verse is sung and the dead are present in the singing. A Khari does not visit a grave. A Khari walks past the place where the body was laid and sings, and the singing is the visit, and the path is the cemetery, and the cemetery is as long as the life the warren has walked.

The thing they carry longest: the silence in the sleeping pile. The space where a body used to be. A canine warren that has lost a member will, for weeks, leave the space unfilled — a gap in the pile that everyone arranges themselves around without discussing it. When the gap eventually closes, because the pile shifts and bodies adjust and life reasserts itself, the grieving Khari will feel it happen in their sleep and wake up, briefly, with the understanding that the loss has been absorbed into the warren's living shape. This is not healing. It is integration. The Khari do not heal from death. They walk through it, carrying it in song, until the walking and the singing are the same thing.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Khari**

Khari children belong to the warren. Not metaphorically — the parental structure is communal from birth. The biological parents are known and acknowledged, but every adult in the warren feeds, teaches, disciplines, and sleeps beside every child. A Khari child who falls and scrapes a knee will be picked up by whoever is nearest, and "whoever is nearest" may be a parent, an aunt, a cousin, or an unrelated warren-mate who joined two seasons ago. The child does not distinguish. The warren does not require them to.

This produces children who are, by settled-race standards, fearlessly social. A Khari five-year-old will walk up to a stranger in a market town and start a conversation. Not because they're naive — because the concept of a stranger is, to a warren-raised child, theoretically interesting and practically meaningless. Everyone they've ever known has been part of the group. A new person is just someone the group hasn't met yet.

The games are lineage-inflected. Canine children play chase-and-wrestle from the moment they can run — physical, joyful, and frequently resulting in the kind of minor injuries that canine warren parents treat with a lick and a shove back into the game. Feline children play solo tracking games — following a scent trail, finding a hidden object, stalking a sibling through the underbrush with a silence that would impress a professional scout. Avian children race. Straight up. The first child to reach the top of the tallest tree or the highest cliff face wins, and the competitive intensity of an avian eight-year-old is genuinely frightening to observe from below.

What they get in trouble for: breaking the path-song. A Khari child learning the warren's songs is given extraordinary latitude — wrong notes, mangled lyrics, and creative reinterpretation are all tolerated with amused patience. But a child who deliberately changes a path-song — who substitutes wrong information as a joke, who alters a route-marker's meaning for fun — is corrected with an immediacy and a seriousness that stands out against the Khari's otherwise relaxed parenting. The phrase every Khari child hears: **"Renn-kha voss, dosset suur"** — "the path doesn't lie, neither do you." The path-song is the warren's memory, its navigation, its survival. Corrupting it, even in play, is corrupting the thing that keeps the family alive. Children learn this early. They learn it once. They do not forget it.

The rite of passage varies by lineage but shares a common structure: the first solo journey. At the warren-appropriate age — twelve for canine, fourteen for feline, eleven for avian (who have flight as an advantage) — the child is given a route they have never walked and told to return before the warren moves again. They carry nothing but water and their knowledge of the path-songs. What they bring back is a new verse — a description of what they found, encoded in the warren's melodic system, sung to the warren upon return. The verse is evaluated by the eldest. If it is accurate, the child has proven they can carry the knowledge. If it is beautiful as well as accurate, the child has proven they can add to it. The highest honor a returning Khari child can receive is silence from the eldest — not disapproval but awe, the recognition that the new verse is good enough to be absorbed into the path-song without correction. The child who earns that silence walks differently afterward. Not taller. Lighter. The weight of proof has been set down, and what remains is the path.

---

### Rathai (Goblins) — 6% of world population

**Physiology:** Small (3'8" to 4'6"), wiry, oversized hands and ears. Skin from sallow green to muddy yellow. Fast metabolism, quick reflexes, high pain tolerance. Lifespan: 50-60 years.
**Distribution:** Urban, almost exclusively. Largest center: the **Nettleworks** — a sprawling district in Thalveris that was originally a drainage system and is now a city-within-a-city housing roughly two million Goblins.
**Dominion Tendency:** Low average output, high efficiency. A Rathai Armoring user won't coat their whole body — they'll Armor their fingertips to pick a lock.

**Cultural Role:** Engineers, tinkers, scrappers. They didn't invent the Grimoire but they figured out how to mass-produce it cheaply. Indispensable to Masqarat infrastructure. Treated as second-class citizens within it.

**Naming:** Informal and functional. Birth names frequently replaced by **work-names** earned through first significant professional achievement. Chosen by the individual. A Rathai who chooses a pompous work-name will be mocked until they choose a better one. Social calibration through mockery is a cultural institution.

**Food:** **Scrap cuisine** — assembled from whatever's cheapest. Nettleworks specialties: **grease-bread** (flatbread fried in rendered fat), **pipe-soup** (thin broth from vegetable scraps — every vendor different, regulars have violent opinions), **charcoal-sugar rolls** (sweet dough rolled in geothermal powdered charcoal). Chalkwall food vendors are Nettleworks celebrities.

**Art:** Graffiti, percussive music, and **mechanical sculpture** — functional machines built for aesthetic purpose. Famous example: a clock tower in Tinthread sub-district built from decommissioned Grimoire components, maintained personally by builder **Vossken Muul** for twenty-three years.

**Key Holidays:** **The Scrap Festival** (2 days, late autumn) — Day one: identical piles of scrap, twelve hours to build something that WORKS. Day two: public display and vote. Winner receives the **Scrap Crown**. Most prestigious Rathai honor. **The Vent-Watch** (1 night, midwinter) — communal wakefulness monitoring geothermal channels in the Nettleworks' deepest sub-level. Stories, games, music, solidarity.

**Coming-of-Age:** Economic — considered adult when you earn your first independent income (typically ages ten-fourteen). First pay-day celebrated by buying food for your family.

**Death Customs:** Buried beneath the buildings they lived in. Over centuries, the Nettleworks' sub-levels have become layered with Rathai remains. Walking through the Vents is walking over generations of dead. Residents do not find this morbid.

**Marriage:** A **shared project** — the couple builds something together. Displayed in the home. Divorce formalized by dismantling the project.

*"They'll fix it, sell it back to you, and somehow end up owning the building."*

---


#### Humanization — Rathai: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Rathai**

A Rathai cable-splicer in the Nettleworks' Tinthread sub-district wakes at third-bell to the sound of her building breathing. The sound is literal — the geothermal channels that run beneath the Nettleworks' lowest levels push warm air through ventilation shafts that hiss and click as the pressure cycles, and every resident of Tinthread has calibrated their sleep to the rhythm. Her apartment is a hundred and eighty square feet shared with her partner and two children. The younger one is already awake, sitting cross-legged on the floor with a disassembled Grimoire relay, rearranging the rune-lattice components in a pattern that is either brilliant or destructive. She checks. Brilliant. She leaves him to it.

Breakfast is grease-bread — flatbread fried in rendered fat, folded around whatever scraps of vegetable or cured meat are in the kitchen bin. The bread costs two Copper Bits from the vendor at the junction of Tinthread's main corridor and Pipe Street, but she makes her own because the vendor's oil is rancid and life is hard enough without eating rancid oil. She eats standing at the counter because the apartment doesn't have a table — the floor serves as table, workshop, and play area, and the lines between these functions are theoretical. Her partner leaves for the assembly shift at the Grimoire fabrication plant on Level Two, kissing her on the forehead with the distracted efficiency of someone who has performed this gesture four thousand times and means it every time without thinking about it.

She walks to the cable junction where her crew works. The Nettleworks' corridors are narrow, crowded, and layered — walkways stacked three high in the larger throughways, with rope bridges connecting the upper levels and metal ladders bolted to every surface. The air smells of grease, hot stone, solder flux, and pipe-soup from the vendor carts that patrol the corridors on wheeled platforms, each cart a one-person kitchen serving a broth that tastes slightly different from every other cart's broth and inspiring loyalties as fierce as any crew bond. Her cart is the third one on Pipe Street, run by an elderly Rathai named Tessik who has been making the same soup for thirty years and will accept no feedback on the recipe. She buys a bowl. It costs two Bits. It tastes like home.

The cable work is physical — splicing runestone-lattice relay cables in the Nettleworks' communication infrastructure, the system that connects two million people to each other and to the wider Grimoire network. Her hands are scarred from lattice cuts. Her oversized Rathai ears pick up frequency hum that human engineers miss. She works a nine-hour shift with a thirty-minute break at midday, during which she eats cold grease-bread from her pocket and argues with her crew about whose section of the splice is cleaner. The argument is technical, passionate, and ongoing from yesterday. It will continue tomorrow. The splice quality is, objectively, identical. The argument is the point.

She's home by evening. The children have eaten — her older daughter cooked pipe-soup from the family recipe, which is not Tessik's recipe but is, in the daughter's opinion, superior. This opinion will be defended to the death. Dinner is whatever the soup is, plus grease-bread, plus a charcoal-sugar roll that her partner brought home from the bakery near the plant because it's the end of the week and the week was long. She falls asleep to the building's breathing and the sound of her younger child still working on the Grimoire relay, the click of rune-lattice components being fitted and refitted in the dark, the sound of a Rathai child doing what Rathai children do — building something, taking it apart, building it again, better.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Rathai**

The Rathai built the world. Not metaphorically — the infrastructure. The Grimoire network that six billion people use daily is 80% assembled in the Nettleworks. The Wardensea's standard equipment is Rathai-manufactured. The drainage systems, the communication relays, the factory output that keeps the Masqarat's economy running — all of it passes through Rathai hands. The Rathai know this. They say it at the Scrap Festival, in their pipe-soup arguments, in the engineering journals they publish under work-names that the academic establishment cites without acknowledgment. "They need us to build the world. They just don't want us living in it." The grievance is real, documented, and three hundred years old.

And yet the Rathai do not revolt. They do not organize politically. They do not march. The Tessaran Reform Congress — the world's largest political reform movement — has eighteen million subscribers. Fewer than two hundred thousand are Rathai. The race that has the most to gain from institutional change is the race least represented in the movement demanding it. This baffles outsiders and does not baffle the Rathai at all, because the Rathai understand something about power that idealists don't: the Masqarat needs Rathai labor more than the Rathai need the Masqarat's approval. A Rathai general strike would collapse the Grimoire network in a week, crash the banking system in two, and halt Wardensea equipment production in three. Everyone knows this. The Masqarat knows this. The Rathai know the Masqarat knows this. The mutual knowledge is its own form of power — quiet, structural, and more durable than any march.
