The contradiction is that this power has not improved Rathai lives. The Nettleworks is still cramped, underfunded, and treated as a resource extraction zone by the Masqarat's economic planners. The Rathai still earn less than every other race for equivalent work. The structural leverage that the Rathai hold has never been exercised because exercising it would mean stopping work, and stopping work is the one thing a Rathai cannot do. Work is identity. The first pay-day is the coming-of-age. The marriage custom is a shared project. The death custom is burial beneath the workspace. A Rathai who stops working has not made a political statement — they have committed a form of cultural self-annihilation. The Masqarat exploits this. The Rathai know the Masqarat exploits this. The knowing does not help, because the alternative to being exploited for your work is not working, and not working is not a thing a Rathai can be.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Rathai**

What Rathai say about other races in the Nettleworks, over pipe-soup, in the specific register of ironic honesty that the Rathai use for everything:

Humans are the bosses. Not always literally — but the Masqarat is sixty percent human, the Wardensea is sixty percent human, the institutional power that shapes Rathai lives is overwhelmingly human. The Rathai do not resent individual humans. They resent the specific human who signs off on the Nettleworks' maintenance budget every year and has never once visited. The Rathai joke about it. "Humans build the laws. We build the buildings the laws sit in. Guess which one falls down without maintenance." The joke is a threat so gentle it sounds like hospitality.

Gorundai are respected without reservation. The two races share an understanding: you are what you make. A Gorundai smith and a Rathai engineer can sit in a workshop for hours without speaking and come away feeling that they've had a full conversation. The friction is minor and physical — Gorundai are big and Rathai are small, and the logistics of shared workspaces produce a running comedy of oversized tools, undersized doorways, and the eternal question of who designed this bench. "It's not a bench. It's a Gorundai footstool."

Thalessi are the Rathai's most complicated relationship. The Rathai built the Grimoire. The Thalessi archive the knowledge. Both races understand the value of what they've produced. But the Thalessi treat Rathai engineering the way they treat everything — as a subject to study, categorize, and file. A Rathai engineer who reads a Thalessi academic paper about Nettleworks infrastructure will find their own innovations described in passive voice, attributed to "Rathai manufacturing traditions" rather than to the specific person who solved the specific problem. The erasure is gentle, institutional, and constant. The Rathai call it **"velvet scrub"** — being cleaned out of your own history so smoothly you barely notice.

Morventhi are allies. The Undersprawl and the Nettleworks are both civilizations built in spaces the surface world discarded. The Morventhi pay Rathai engineers fairly and treat them as colleagues. This is not complicated. The Rathai like the Morventhi because the Morventhi like the Rathai's work, and liking someone's work is, in Rathai culture, the purest form of respect.

Varrek are the Rathai's mirror. Both races are small, technically minded, and essential to the world's infrastructure. The Varrek design. The Rathai build. The partnership is productive and the rivalry is ancient. A Rathai who discovers that a Varrek patent is based on a Rathai prototype will pursue the claim with a tenacity that makes Wardensea bounty hunters look casual. The Varrek patent system, from the Rathai perspective, is a mechanism for stealing Rathai ideas with better paperwork. The Varrek perspective — that they refine and systematize what the Rathai invent chaotically — is technically accurate and emotionally tone-deaf. The argument funds approximately twelve percent of Gannavar's legal profession.

Khari are trading partners and cultural neighbors. The Nettleworks and the warrens intersect at every port market. The Khari sell raw materials. The Rathai sell finished goods. The exchange is old, reliable, and conducted with the mutual respect of two peoples who both know what it means to be undervalued by the institutions that depend on them. Half-Khari-Rathai children are common and welcomed. The Nettleworks does not discriminate. It needs the hands.

Oni are fun and frightening. A Rathai who encounters an Oni for the first time is confronted with a being roughly twice their height, three times their weight, and ten times their volume, who wants to feed them until they burst and considers this a form of friendship. The Rathai adapt. The Rathai always adapt. But the size differential is a running source of Rathai humor. "I met an Oni. I think they sat on my house."

Lunarians are stories. Most Rathai have never met one. The Nettleworks' oral tradition includes Lunarian figures as characters in children's tales — the luminous stranger who fixes things, who leaves before dawn, who knows how to make anything work. Whether these tales contain actual Lunarian contact or are Rathai wish-fulfillment projected onto a mysterious people is a question that Rathai storytellers consider beneath them. "Does the story work? Then who cares if it's true?"

What other races say about the Rathai: "They're everywhere and you don't see them." This is the most Rathai complaint about the world's perception — not that they're hated or feared, but that they're invisible. A Rathai who works a twelve-hour shift maintaining the communication infrastructure that lets six billion people argue on the Grimoire goes home to a two-hundred-square-foot apartment and reads commentary by people who have never once wondered how the signal reaches their device. The invisibility is the injury. The work continues anyway. The work always continues. That's the Rathai.

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Rathai**

A Rathai in love fixes things. Not as a metaphor — as a literal, physical behavior. A Rathai who's attracted to someone will notice what's broken in their life and repair it without being asked. The Grimoire with the cracked casing. The pipe-soup cart with the wobbly wheel. The cable splice that's been buzzing for a week. A Rathai suitor shows up, fixes the thing, and leaves. If the other person notices, that's the opening. If they don't, the Rathai fixes something else. The courtship is a series of unsolicited repairs that escalates in complexity until the other person either says "why do you keep fixing my things?" (at which point the Rathai says "because I want to," which is the declaration) or doesn't notice (at which point the Rathai moves on, slightly wounded, and channels the disappointment into a project).

The shared project of marriage is not a symbol. It is a diagnostic. A Rathai couple that can build something together — negotiate design choices, divide labor, tolerate each other's methods, and produce a result they're both willing to display in their home — is a couple that can survive the Nettleworks. The project is typically small: a clock, a lamp, a piece of mechanical sculpture. What matters is the process. A couple that argues productively during the project will argue productively during the marriage. A couple that can't agree on bolt gauge will not survive the first year. The Rathai consider this system more reliable than any emotional assessment. Feelings change. Bolt gauge doesn't.

Rathai tenderness is industrial. A Rathai partner who brings home a charcoal-sugar roll at the end of a long week is performing an act of love so compressed that an outsider might miss it entirely. A Gorundai partner makes grand gestures. A Thalessi partner constructs elaborate emotional architectures. A Rathai partner remembers that you like the sugar rolls from the bakery near the plant and that Friday is when you need one most, and they carry it home in their pocket because their hands are full of tools, and they put it on the counter without comment, and the absence of comment is the comment — I see you. I know what you need. I carried it.

Rathai grief is quiet because Rathai lives are loud.

When a Rathai dies, the work pauses. Not ceremonially — practically. Someone was doing something, and now someone isn't, and the gap is felt in the workflow before it's felt in the heart. The first response to a Rathai death in the Nettleworks is logistical: who covers the shift? Who takes the tools? Who tells the family? The logistics are not callousness. They are the structure that holds the grief. A Rathai who is given a task in the hours after a death is a Rathai who has something to do with their hands, and a Rathai with something to do with their hands can survive anything.

The burial is beneath the building. The body goes into the sub-levels, into the layered earth that is already thick with the Rathai dead of centuries. No marker. No stone. The building itself is the marker — the building the dead person helped maintain, or built, or wired, or patched. A Rathai who dies in the Nettleworks is buried beneath their own work, and every step taken on the floor above them is a footstep on a memorial that no one needs to read because the memorial is the floor itself, and the floor works, and working is remembering.

The first pay-day after the death is the mourning. Not formally — no one declares it. But the Rathai who has lost someone will take their first paycheck after the loss and buy food for the family, the same gesture that marked their coming-of-age, the same gesture that said "I am a person who provides." Doing it again, in grief, is not a repetition. It is a declaration: the work continues. The family eats. The building stands. Underneath the building, the dead are still. Above them, the living are building.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Rathai**

Rathai children work. Not as exploitation — as identity. A Rathai child in the Nettleworks is expected to contribute to the household by age eight, and by twelve most have taken on paid work: running messages, assembling Grimoire components, repairing small devices, serving pipe-soup from a vendor cart. The line between childhood and labor is, by settled-race standards, shockingly thin. By Rathai standards, it doesn't exist. A child who works is a child who learns. A child who learns is a child who survives. The Nettleworks does not have the luxury of extended adolescence.

This does not mean Rathai children are joyless. The games are inventive, fast, and always involve building something. **Scrap-race** — each child gets an identical pile of salvaged materials and thirty minutes to build something that moves — is the junior version of the Scrap Festival, and the competition is genuinely fierce. A Rathai child who wins a neighborhood scrap-race earns a social currency that no adult appointment can match. The winning designs are displayed on the corridor walls. Some stay up for years. A twenty-year-old scrap-race trophy pinned to a Nettleworks wall tells you that a child built something remarkable in that spot, and the community thought it was worth remembering.

What they get in trouble for: waste. Not the moral abstraction — the physical act. A Rathai child who throws away something usable will hear about it with a velocity and intensity that makes Wardensea disciplinary proceedings look relaxed. The phrase every Rathai child hears: **"Doss keth vrenn"** — "nothing is nothing." Meaning: everything is something. The scrap is a component. The broken device is parts. The leftover food is tomorrow's soup. Waste is not an economic failing — it is a moral one, because waste means someone in the Nettleworks goes without, and going without is not theoretical in a district where eight percent of the population is destitute. A Rathai child learns to see the potential in discarded things before they learn to read, and this vision — the ability to look at garbage and see a machine — is the foundation of everything the Rathai build.

The coming-of-age is the first pay-day. The age varies — ten to fourteen — and the milestone is economic, not physical or ceremonial. The child earns their first independent income. The first money goes to buying food for the family. This is not optional. It is the gesture that turns a child into a provider, and the family receives it with the same weight that a Gorundai family gives the First Challenge or a Thalessi family gives the Naming Ceremony. The first meal bought by the child is eaten together, and the quality of the food is irrelevant — it could be grease-bread and pipe-soup, it could be charcoal-sugar rolls, it could be the cheapest thing at the cheapest cart. What matters is that the child earned it and the family ate it. The exchange is economic and sacred and the same thing.

After the first pay-day, the child earns a work-name. Chosen by the individual, subject to community approval. A child who picks a name too grand is mocked. A child who picks a name too humble is mocked differently. The calibration is social, instant, and merciless. The name that survives the mockery is the name that fits. Most Rathai adults remember their work-name negotiation — the three days of trying options, the laughter, the moment when the right name landed and the laughter stopped and someone nodded and said "that's you." The nod is the ceremony. The ceremony is enough.

---

That's the Rathai. Moving to **Varrek**.

### Varrek (Gnomes) — 5% of world population

**Physiology:** Small (3'4" to 4'2"), stocky, broad hands. Slightly enlarged cranial capacity. Lifespan: 90-110 years.
**Distribution:** Clustered around centers of learning. Homeland: **Gannavar**, Masqarat-allied city-state on Orenthas's southern peninsula — engineering academies, patent courts.
**Dominion Tendency:** Vision-dominant, cerebral flavor — experienced as data streams rather than sensory expansion.

**Cultural Role:** Scholars, inventors, systematizers. Grimoire communication protocol is Varrek-designed. Wardensea charts are Varrek-maintained. Kolmari banking runs on Varrek calculation engines. They make themselves so useful that conquering them is worse than cooperating.

**Naming:** Compound and precise — constructed from meaningful elements describing family profession, city, or specialization. Varrek consider names tools — they should communicate information.

**Food:** Engineered. **Calibrated porridge** — grain-based, exact specifications (92°C, 1:3.2 ratio, 11 minutes). Tastes adequate. Consistency is perfect. The Varrek find this satisfying. Other races find this depressing. Their preserved foods (jams, pickles, smoked meats) are excellent trade commodities.

**Art:** Technical illustration and precision metalwork. The Varrek word for "beautiful" and "accurate" is the same: **verath**. This tells you everything.

**Key Holidays:** **Patent Day** (1 day, spring) — Gannavar's Central Exchange opens archives for public viewing. Year's most significant new patent read aloud; audience applauds, silences, or laughs. All three have occurred simultaneously. **The Measure** (1 evening, autumn equinox) — each family member reports one thing they measured, built, or quantified. Youngest first, eldest last. No applause, no evaluation.

**Coming-of-Age:** At age twenty, present a **first patent** to the family. Evaluated with professional rigor. Kindness is not part of the process.

**Death Customs:** Buried with their finest work — the object they consider their best creation placed in the coffin. Funeral obituary lists patents, publications, professional contributions.

**Marriage:** A **joint patent** filed with the Central Exchange. Legally binding, publicly accessible. Romantic love assumed but not documented.

*"They'll measure the world twice before they touch it once."*

---


#### Humanization — Varrek: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Varrek**

A Varrek hydrologist in Gannavar wakes at exactly the same time every morning because he has measured his sleep cycles and determined that seven hours and twenty-two minutes produces optimal cognitive function for his specific physiology. The alarm is a Grimoire device of his own modification — quieter than the commercial model, calibrated to a frequency that triggers wakefulness without cortisol spike. He is proud of this modification. He has described it to colleagues. Colleagues have stopped asking.

Breakfast is calibrated porridge. Ninety-two degrees, one-to-three-point-two ratio of water to grain, eleven minutes of cook time. He has eaten this porridge every morning for nineteen years. He has never considered changing it because the porridge satisfies all nutritional requirements for the morning and changing it would require recalibration, and the time spent recalibrating would be better spent on hydrology. His wife eats the same porridge with the addition of preserved plum, which he considers an unnecessary variable and she considers the only reason the meal is edible. This disagreement is nineteen years old and precisely as unresolved as the porridge. Both consider this acceptable.

The walk to the Central Hydrology Bureau takes fourteen minutes through Gannavar's gridded streets — every intersection measured, every building numbered, every drainage grate catalogued in a municipal database maintained by Varrek who consider municipal drainage a calling rather than a job. He passes the Patent Exchange, where the morning's queue of inventors is already forming to file new patents or challenge existing ones. He passes the Central Library, which is open twenty-two hours a day because the Varrek could not agree on whether to close for six or eight hours and compromised on two. He passes the bakery that sells calibrated porridge in takeaway containers for Varrek who are too busy to cook, and he notes, as he does every morning, that their water-to-grain ratio is off by 0.1. He has mentioned this once. He will mention it again.

At the Bureau, his work is water. Gannavar's water management system — the network of pipes, filters, reservoirs, and drainage channels that serves two hundred thousand people — is Varrek-designed, Varrek-maintained, and the subject of more internal memos than any other topic in the city's administrative history. He reads the overnight flow reports. He adjusts two valves. He writes a memo about the adjustment, copying it to three departments, because undocumented adjustments are, in Varrek professional culture, equivalent to sabotage. His afternoon is a meeting about a pipe junction that has been performing at 97.3% efficiency for six months. The meeting's purpose: determining whether 97.3% is acceptable or whether the 2.7% loss requires intervention. The meeting will last two hours. It has been scheduled for two hours. It will end exactly on time because Varrek meetings end on time, and the fact that the decision will not be reached today means another meeting will be scheduled, and the scheduling of that meeting will take eleven minutes.

He is home by evening. Dinner is his wife's preparation — roasted vegetables, smoked meat, a precision that mirrors his own but with better seasoning, which he attributes to her superior palate and she attributes to his inferior willingness to deviate from a recipe. The evening is reading: a hydrology journal, a patent filing he's reviewing for a colleague, and twelve minutes of a Grimoire serial about a Renegade crew that his wife follows and he pretends not to. He falls asleep at exactly the time his measured cycle requires, to the sound of Gannavar's water system — a low, constant hum of flowing water through pipes that he personally calibrated, and the sound is not soothing in the way that music is soothing. It is soothing in the way that a correct answer is soothing. The water flows at the rate it should. The system works. He can sleep.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Varrek**

The Varrek believe that knowledge should be free. This is not a policy position — it is a civilizational principle. The Gallavan Principle, named for Brenoch Gallavan, states that knowledge hoarded by authority is knowledge used as a weapon. The Varrek built the Grimoire communication protocol specifically to distribute information. They maintain the world's most accessible patent archives. They publish research openly. They consider the Masqarat's Second Age suppression the single greatest institutional crime in history — not because of the moral dimension (though they acknowledge it) but because suppressing knowledge is, to a Varrek, functionally insane. You don't burn a blueprint. You don't erase a measurement. You don't destroy data because the data is inconvenient. The Varrek horror at the Erasure Clerks is not political. It is visceral, the way a musician's horror at a smashed instrument is visceral.

And yet the Varrek patent system — the institution they are most proud of — is a mechanism for restricting access to knowledge. A patent is, by definition, the legal right to prevent others from using an idea you had first. The Varrek do not see this as a contradiction because they distinguish between "knowledge" (which is free) and "application" (which is owned). The formula is published. The device you build from the formula is yours. The distinction is philosophically coherent and practically exploitative — because the Varrek, who are better resourced and better educated than the Rathai engineers who often originate the ideas, file the patents first. The Gallavan Principle says knowledge should be free. The Gannavar patent office says: free to read, expensive to use.

The Rathai know this. They have always known this. The twelve percent of Gannavar's legal profession funded by Rathai patent challenges is the slow, ongoing argument about whether the Varrek actually believe their own principle or whether the principle is a cover for intellectual extraction dressed in generosity.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Varrek**

What Varrek say about other races in Gannavar's meeting rooms, patent offices, and the precise, clinical social gatherings that the Varrek call "dinners" and everyone else calls "conferences":

Humans are imprecise. Not stupid — imprecise. They approximate when they should measure. They estimate when they should calculate. A Varrek who works alongside a human team will spend the first week re-measuring everything the humans have done and the second week trying to explain why the re-measurement was necessary. The humans find this insulting. The Varrek find the inaccuracy insulting. Neither side can see the other's injury. The saving grace: humans are fast. What they lack in precision they compensate for with a speed of execution that the Varrek find both impressive and terrifying. "They built the entire port in three months. Half the measurements are wrong, but they BUILT it."

Thalessi are the Varrek's intellectual peers, which makes them the Varrek's primary competitors. The Collegium Verossian produces research that the Varrek must acknowledge and the acknowledgment tastes like copper. The friction is methodological: the Thalessi approach knowledge through observation and long-term pattern recognition. The Varrek approach it through measurement and systematic testing. Both methods work. Both methods produce different conclusions from the same data with alarming regularity. Joint publications between Thalessi and Varrek scholars are considered the gold standard of academic work and require approximately three times longer to produce because of the methodological arguments.

Gorundai are respected through gritted teeth. The Gorundai make the best weapons in the world by feel. This sentence alone is enough to give a Varrek an anxiety response. "By FEEL. They forge Master-grade Resonance weapons by the COLOR OF THE HEAT." The Varrek know that Gorundai-Varrek forge teams produce the finest results. They know that the Varrek contribution — acoustic measurement, frequency calibration, metallurgical analysis — is essential. They also know that the Gorundai contribution — a sixty-year-old smith who looks at the metal and says "another thirty seconds" based on nothing measurable — is equally essential. This drives them slightly mad. It is the single greatest proof that their worldview is incomplete, and they will never admit it.

Rathai are the wound. The Varrek know — and have always known — that Rathai engineers originate a disproportionate number of the innovations that end up in Varrek patent filings. The relationship is collaborative on paper and extractive in practice. The Varrek who are honest about this (a growing minority, concentrated in Gannavar's younger generation) consider the patent system's treatment of Rathai innovators the single largest ethical failure in Varrek institutional history. The Varrek who are not honest about it (the majority, concentrated in the patent office administration) argue that systematizing and refining a Rathai prototype constitutes original contribution. The argument maps exactly onto the Gallavan Principle's tensions. The Varrek are aware of the irony. Awareness has not produced change.

Morventhi are admired for their infrastructure. The Undersprawl is, from a Varrek engineering perspective, a masterwork — seventeen levels carved from living stone, ventilated, heated, and drained with a precision that the Varrek find professionally beautiful. The Morventhi achieved this without Varrek assistance for the first two thousand years, which the Varrek find both admirable and, quietly, threatening. The idea that someone built something this well without measurement is a philosophical challenge they prefer not to examine too closely.

Khari are data sources. This framing is unflattering and accurate. The Varrek value Khari path-songs as navigational data of extraordinary accuracy and have spent centuries trying to convert the melodic format into measurable coordinates. The conversion works approximately eighty percent of the time. The twenty percent it doesn't work is the twenty percent where the song encodes something the Varrek instruments don't measure — wind-feel, current-sense, the intuitive reading of an environment. The Varrek response to this twenty percent is to build better instruments. The Khari response is to laugh.

Oni are large, loud, and statistically fascinating. A Varrek who encounters an Oni for the first time will, within ten minutes, be mentally estimating their caloric intake, bone density, and horn tensile strength. They cannot help this. The Measure Fruit that Ressaven Delvuun carries is, in some ways, just the externalized version of what every Varrek does internally. Oni find this endearing when they notice it and alarming when they realize the Varrek has been calculating their weight since the handshake.

Lunarians are the Varrek's most painful case. The Varrek believe knowledge should be free. The Lunarians carry knowledge that the Masqarat suppresses. The Varrek should, by their own principle, be the Lunarians' most passionate defenders. They are not. Gannavar has accepted three Lunarian refugees in the past century and filed no formal protests against the Masqarat's census program. The Gallavan Principle applies to knowledge. Applying it to people costs more, and the Varrek — for all their principle — have not paid that cost. They know this. It sits in the same drawer as the patent system's treatment of the Rathai: acknowledged, unresolved, and left alone because resolving it would require the Varrek to choose between their principles and their comfort, and the Varrek have always been better at measuring things than at breaking them.

What other races say about the Varrek: "They'd measure the sunset before they watched it." The Varrek hear this and wonder why you'd watch a sunset without measuring it. The measurement IS the watching. The angle of light. The rate of atmospheric color shift. The precise moment the disc touches the horizon. A Varrek who watches a sunset without noting the data feels like they've wasted the sunset. Other races find this tragic. The Varrek find other races' unsystematic appreciation of beauty equally tragic. The impasse is permanent.

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Varrek**

A Varrek in love measures more. This is not a joke — it is the first symptom. A Varrek who is developing feelings for someone will begin unconsciously tracking their patterns: when they arrive at work, how they take their tea, the frequency of their laughter, the specific way they hold their pen. The Varrek will not recognize this behavior as attraction until the dataset is large enough to produce a conclusion, at which point the Varrek will review the data, confirm the hypothesis, and proceed to courtship with the same methodical precision they apply to everything else. A Varrek who tells someone "I've noticed that your arrival time correlates with my improved productivity" is not being clinical. They are being romantic. The correlation IS the confession.

The courtship is intellectual. Competence is the trigger — watching someone solve a problem well is the Varrek's most reliable aphrodisiac. The Gannavar term **verath-kindled** ("accuracy-sparked") is used unironically, and a Varrek who says "your methodology was elegant" at a conference dinner is, in the privacy of their own emotional landscape, flirting at maximum intensity. The progression from professional admiration to personal interest is gradual, documented (usually unconsciously), and culminates in the Varrek equivalent of a grand gesture: the joint patent.

A joint patent filed with the Central Exchange is the Varrek marriage. It is a legal document. It is publicly accessible. It contains the couple's combined intellectual contribution to an original work, and the work itself is the proof that their minds are compatible. A Varrek couple's joint patent is examined by the community with the same scrutiny that a Gorundai community applies to the shared scar — not for the formality but for the quality. A brilliant joint patent means a brilliant partnership. An adequate joint patent raises quiet concerns. A terrible joint patent — one that shows fundamental incompatibility of method — produces the Varrek equivalent of a raised eyebrow, which is the most devastating form of social commentary available to a people who do not gossip so much as audit.

Varrek love, once established, is maintenance. Not in the pejorative sense — in the engineering sense. A Varrek partner identifies what works, preserves it, and monitors it for degradation. They remember anniversaries not through sentiment but through scheduling. They notice when their partner's mood shifts not through emotional intuition but through behavioral pattern recognition. The result, from the outside, looks mechanical. From the inside, it is attentive in a way that more expressive cultures rarely achieve, because a Varrek who has measured their partner's happiness is a Varrek who can reproduce the conditions of that happiness with scientific accuracy. The love is in the measurement. The measurement is constant.

Varrek grief is data loss.

This is not a metaphor. A Varrek who loses someone experiences the loss as an interruption in a dataset they had been building for years or decades — the ongoing record of a person's patterns, habits, responses, and contributions to the Varrek's own life. The dataset stops updating. The most recent entry becomes the last entry. The Varrek mind, which is trained to project trends from data, keeps extrapolating beyond the final point and producing predictions that will never be verified, and the inability to verify is the grief.

The funeral places the dead person's finest work in the coffin. The obituary lists patents, publications, professional contributions. This is not the Varrek reducing a life to a resume — it is the Varrek honoring the thing they value most: what you made. A Varrek obituary that lists seventeen patents and a breakthrough hydrology model is saying "this person changed the shape of what we know, and the shape persists after they do not." The persistence of the work is the closest thing to immortality the Varrek believe in, and they believe in it completely.

A year after the death, the Varrek's grief has been integrated into the daily routine the way a compensating variable is integrated into a system — noted, adjusted for, and carried forward. The dead person's work sits in the patent archive, accessible, permanent, and the Varrek who loved them visits it the way another race might visit a grave: to read the document, to remember the methodology, to feel, in the precision of the work, the presence of the person who produced it. A Varrek touching a dead partner's patent filing is not reading a document. They are holding a hand.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Varrek**

Varrek children measure things before they can fully articulate what they're measuring. A Varrek toddler with a piece of string will measure the string against every object in the room — not because they've been taught to but because the impulse to quantify is, in Varrek children, as instinctive as the impulse to grab. By three, they're sorting objects by size. By five, they're asking "how much" and "how many" with a frequency that drives non-Varrek caretakers to distraction. A Varrek parent hears these questions and answers them with exact figures, because approximate answers given to a Varrek child produce a facial expression of betrayal that no parent can bear more than twice.

The games are structured. **Count-race** — a speed competition where children are given a complex scene and must identify the number of distinct objects faster than their opponents — is the standard childhood obsession. The game appears simple. The strategy is not: children learn to group, estimate, and verify in rapid sequence, developing the pattern-recognition skills that will define their professional lives. A Varrek child who wins a count-race tournament is considered promising. A Varrek child who wins by developing a novel counting method is considered gifted. The novel method is documented. Sometimes it ends up in the patent archive, filed under the child's name, before they're ten.

What they get in trouble for: inaccuracy. Not lying — a Varrek child who lies is corrected, but the Varrek consider deliberate deception a known variable, manageable and finite. Inaccuracy is worse because it implies that the child measured badly, and bad measurement is the foundation of bad everything. The phrase every Varrek child hears: **"Verath kenn dosset"** — "accuracy before opinion." Meaning: you can think what you want, but your measurements must be correct, because the world does not care about your interpretation — it cares about your data. Every Varrek adult recites this from memory. Every Varrek adult also remembers the humiliation of presenting a school project with a measurement error and watching their teacher correct it in front of the class with the quiet, surgical precision that the Varrek consider appropriate educational methodology and every other race considers psychologically scarring.

The first patent at twenty is the rite of passage. The family evaluates it with professional rigor. Kindness is not part of the process. A Varrek parent who praises a mediocre first patent is doing their child a disservice that borders on cruelty, because the world beyond the family will not be kind, and a Varrek who enters professional life with an inflated sense of their own accuracy is a Varrek who is about to be destroyed by peer review. The evaluation is honest. Sometimes it is devastating. The child who hears "this is adequate" from a Varrek parent has received the highest available praise. The child who hears "revise and resubmit" has received the most common outcome. The child who hears "the methodology is sound" has received something rarer and more valuable than either — the acknowledgment that their mind works correctly, even if the execution needs refinement. That distinction — sound method, imperfect result — is the difference between a Varrek child who will thrive and a Varrek child who will merely produce.

---

That's the Varrek. Now the **Humans**.

### Lunarians — ~20,000 total worldwide

**Physiology:** White or silver hair, faintly luminescent pale skin, functional feathered wings spanning 8-12 feet. Can fly (sustained flight exhausting, rarely over an hour). Low-grade thermal field from upper back. Lifespan: unknown with certainty — oldest confirmed living Lunarian is 210.
**Distribution:** Scattered. Small communities of 50-200 in isolated mountain settlements. Handful in Vethara under cultural asylum. Unknown number in hiding under false identities (wing-binding practiced). The Masqarat maintains a quiet census. The Lunarians know about the census.
**Dominion Tendency:** Extraordinary Vision — documented perceiving events up to ten seconds into the future, triple the human maximum.

**Cultural Role:** A dying people who remember things the Masqarat has spent three thousand years trying to erase. Their oral history claims they once numbered in the millions. The Masqarat's position: "naturally rare species." Lunarian elders disagree but will not say more. They have been asked. They have been tortured. They do not break.

**Naming:** Silverborn conventions — long, flowing, multi-syllabic. Unchanged in 3,000 years. Child's name chosen through **the Listening** — parents sit in silence until a name presents itself. Connected to Hettashi's memory-preservation function.

**Art:** **Wing-feather weaving** (shed feathers, never plucked) — textiles finer than silk, iridescent. Never sold. Given as gifts of extraordinary trust. **Memory-singing** — complex compositions encoding historical records in musical form. Each community maintains a **living song** sung continuously for generations, the oldest estimated to contain material from within a century of the genocide.

**Key Holidays:** **The Remembering** (when a memory-keeper dies) — the most sacred observance. The dying keeper speaks their fragment of Solmund's archive; a trained successor memorizes and repeats word-perfect. If the keeper dies before transfer is complete, the lost portion is gone forever. Two fragments have been partially lost in 3,000 years. **The Binding** (1 night, annual) — communities remove wing-bindings collectively and fly. For some, the first time in months. "Coming home to a body you forgot you had." At dawn, they bind again.

**Coming-of-Age:** **The First Carrying** at twenty-five — entrusted with a small piece of community knowledge to memorize and recite perfectly after one year.

**Death Customs:** Sky burial. Body carried to the highest point, left exposed. Bones collected and placed in communal cairn. Wing-feathers harvested for weaving.

*"Wings like angels. Eyes like they've already watched you die."*

---


#### Humanization — Lunarians: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Lunarians**

A Lunarian memory-keeper in a mountain settlement of eighty wakes before the others because the Listening requires silence, and silence in a Lunarian community lasts only until the first child stirs. Her settlement sits at six thousand feet in a range that no cartographer has named because naming it would put it on a map, and maps are read by people who track Lunarians. Eighty people. Four buildings, stone-walled, built into the slope so that the rooflines match the ridge and cannot be seen from below. The architecture of invisibility is the first thing a Lunarian child learns about their home: we are here, and we are not found, and these two facts are the same fact.

Breakfast is mountain food — dried goat, root vegetables stored in a cold-cave, flatbread baked on a stone oven that every settlement has and that every settlement builds to the same design, passed down from a time when the Silverborn built with stone and the recipes have not changed because changing them would mean forgetting the original shape. She eats with the other early risers — the watch-keeper who has spent the night scanning the slopes with Vision that can perceive ten seconds into the future and who uses this extraordinary gift to ensure that nothing approaches the settlement while its people sleep. The watch-keeper eats slowly. The memory-keeper eats quickly. Both know why the other's pace is what it is.

The morning is the Listening. Not the formal Listening that names children — the daily Listening, the quiet practice of holding the fragment of Solmund's archive in active memory and reviewing it for degradation. The memory-keeper sits alone in a room built for this purpose — stone walls, no windows, a single bioluminescent lichen on the ceiling that the community has maintained for generations because it produces the specific quality of light that Lunarian eyes evolved to read by. She speaks her fragment aloud, once, completely. The fragment is fourteen minutes long. It contains historical material that the Masqarat would classify as a capital offense to possess. She has carried it for forty years. She will carry it until she dies or until she trains a successor to carry it after her.

The afternoon is community work — tending the garden (high-altitude crops that grow slowly and taste like effort), repairing stone walls, maintaining the water channel from the spring above, and the constant, low-grade labor of keeping eighty people alive in a place that is deliberately difficult to reach. A Lunarian settlement is not self-sufficient by choice — it is self-sufficient by necessity, because dependence on the outside world means contact with the outside world, and contact means the census, and the census means the Masqarat knows where you are.

Evening is the wing-hour. Between dusk and dark, when the light is too dim for ground-watchers to see clearly, several members of the community unbind their wings and fly. Not far — the settlement's perimeter, a slow circuit, the stretch and ache of muscles that spend most of their existence compressed against the back under bindings designed to make the wings invisible beneath clothing. The thermal field from the upper back increases during flight, and a Lunarian in the air glows faintly in the deepening dark, and the glow is the most beautiful and most dangerous thing about them. They land before full dark. They bind again. The children who watched from the ground are quiet in the way that children are quiet when they've seen something they want and can't have yet.

She falls asleep to the wind. At six thousand feet, the wind is constant and sharp and carries the sound of nothing — no city, no harbor, no geothermal hum, no corridor traffic. Just the mountain and the air and the specific silence of a people who have learned to live in the gaps the world does not look at, and the silence is not peace. It is the sound of being hidden, and being hidden is the sound of being hunted, and the memory-keeper carries both in her fragment and in her sleep and in the fact that she wakes before dawn because the Listening requires silence, and silence is the only thing her people have left that is entirely their own.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Lunarians**

The Lunarians carry the world's memory. This is their purpose — the inheritance of the Silverborn, the fragments of Solmund's archive distributed across communities so small that losing a single memory-keeper means losing a piece of the past that cannot be recovered. The Lunarians protect this memory with their lives. They have been tortured for it. They have died for it. The Masqarat has spent three thousand years trying to erase it and the Lunarians have spent three thousand years refusing to let it go. This commitment is real, absolute, and the defining characteristic of Lunarian culture.

And the Lunarians do not share it. The memory they protect — the Second Age truth, the genocide's record, the Silverborn script that the Masqarat calls an ontological hazard — sits in the minds of memory-keepers who will not speak it to outsiders. Kessendra Voluun has spent eighteen years assembling fragments of Second Age truth from oral tradition, ruin-reading, and intercepted communications. She has never asked a Lunarian memory-keeper directly, because Lunarian memory-keepers do not speak to outsiders about the archive. The Tessaran Reform Congress, which campaigns for historical truth, has never received Lunarian cooperation. The Khari, whose oral tradition is the closest cultural cousin to the Lunarian's, have never been offered a fragment.

The Lunarians preserve the truth and deny the world access to it. The justification is safety — sharing the archive means exposing it to Masqarat interception, and the archive's survival depends on its secrecy. This is true. It is also a choice that has allowed the Masqarat's suppression to stand unchallenged for three thousand years, because the people who hold the proof refuse to show it. The Lunarians are the keepers of the light who have chosen to keep it in the dark, and the question of whether the keeping has become the point — whether the Lunarians have confused the preservation of truth with the possession of it — is a question that no Lunarian elder will answer and no Lunarian younger generation has yet dared to ask.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Lunarians**

Lunarians do not speak about other races the way other races speak about each other. Eighty people in a mountain settlement do not generate the kind of cultural discourse that six hundred million Thalveris residents produce. What the Lunarians carry is older and quieter: a knowledge of what each race did during the Second Age, held in fragments of Solmund's archive, coloring every interaction a Lunarian has with the outside world.

They know what the humans did. The administrative backbone of the genocide. Most humans don't know. The Lunarians do. Every human a Lunarian meets is filtered through this knowledge — not with hatred, but with an asymmetry that corrodes from the inside. The human is friendly. The Lunarian is polite. The human does not know what the Lunarian is remembering. The Lunarian cannot forget.

They know what the Thalessi promised and didn't deliver. The archives that were supposed to be shared and weren't. The protection that was offered and withdrawn. A Lunarian in Vethara under cultural asylum is grateful and watchful in equal measure, because the Thalessi are being generous now, and generosity revoked is worse than hostility sustained.

They know the Morventhi chose to descend and did not invite the Silverborn's descendants to descend with them. The Undersprawl was carved for the Vetha-moren. The Lunarians were not included. Three thousand years later, the Morventhi treat Lunarians with reverence, and the Lunarians accept the reverence and remember the exclusion.

They know the Gorundai fought. That during the worst of it, certain Gorundai clans refused to participate and sheltered Silverborn refugees at the cost of their own safety. This knowledge survives in the archive. The modern Gorundai do not know which clans sheltered and which didn't. The Lunarians do. When a Lunarian meets a Gorundai whose clan name matches a sheltering clan, something in the Lunarian's expression shifts — not visible to non-Vision users, but warm, brief, and never explained.

The Khari are the closest thing to allies the Lunarians have among the mortal races, because the Khari remember the Silverborn in their own way — the path-songs, the "luminous stranger" tales — and the overlap between Khari and Lunarian oral tradition is a bridge the two peoples have walked quietly for centuries. A Khari warren that encounters a Lunarian community will share information freely, ask no questions that aren't offered, and leave path-markers that, read correctly, indicate "friends here" in a way that only other Khari can decode.

What other races say about the Lunarians: nothing, mostly. Twenty thousand people in a world of six billion are easy to forget. When they are remembered, the image is wings and light and sadness — a dying people who glow in the dark and know things they won't say. The image is romantic and incomplete. The Lunarians are not tragic. They are strategic. Every choice they make — the hiding, the binding, the silence, the refusal to share — is a calculated decision to survive in a world that tried to erase them and has not yet stopped trying. The glow is not beauty. It is a target. The binding is not submission. It is armor. The silence is not peace. It is the sound of a people who have decided that existing is a form of defiance, and defiance does not require volume.

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Lunarians**

Lunarian love begins with the wings.

The wings are more intimate than nudity. A Lunarian who removes their bindings in another person's presence has said, without words, everything that needs to be said. The thermal field from the upper back intensifies with emotion — arousal, trust, vulnerability — and the glow that results is visible in any low light, broadcasting a state that the Lunarian cannot control and cannot hide. A Lunarian who is falling in love glows brighter, and the brightness is a confession their body makes before their mind consents.

Courtship in a community of eighty is not like courtship in a city of a million. Everyone knows. The settlement is too small for secrets. When two Lunarians begin orbiting each other — the specific gravity of attraction pulling them into shared tasks, shared meals, shared watches — the community adjusts around them with a quiet, practiced ease that suggests this choreography has been performed many times. Space is made. Privacy is offered without being requested, which is notable in a community where privacy barely exists. The offering is the community's blessing, given wordlessly, because Lunarian culture has learned that some things are sacred precisely because they are not announced.

The unbinding — the first time a Lunarian shows their wings to the person they love — is not a formal ceremony. It happens in private, in the wing-hour or later, in the dark where the glow is visible and the wings catch what light there is. The partner sees what the world does not: the full Lunarian, uncompressed, wings spread, thermal field radiating, the inheritance of the Silverborn visible in the span and the light. What the partner does in that moment — whether they reach for the wings, whether they stand still, whether they meet the glow with their own — defines the relationship. The Lunarians do not talk about this. It is the one act more private than the Morventhi's Deep Night, and its privacy is guarded with the same absolute silence.

Lunarian grief is the Remembering.

When a memory-keeper dies, the most sacred observance begins: the dying keeper speaks their fragment of Solmund's archive, and a trained successor memorizes and repeats it word-perfect. If the keeper dies before the transfer is complete, the lost portion is gone forever. Two fragments have been partially lost in three thousand years. The grief for the person and the grief for the knowledge are inseparable, and the inseparability is the cruelest thing about Lunarian death — you cannot mourn the dead without calculating what they took with them, and the calculation is not callous. It is the responsibility the living owe to the dead's burden.

For non-keepers — a partner, a parent, a child — the grief is the sky burial. The body carried to the highest point. Left for the wind and the weather. Wing-feathers harvested by the family for weaving, the shed becoming a textile that is finer than silk, iridescent, and carried as a garment or a blanket that the grieving partner will wrap themselves in for years, because the feather-weaving smells of the thermal field and the thermal field smells of the person, and the scent is the last physical trace and it fades slowly, and the fading is the grief's clock, and when the scent is gone the grief is not over but the body's last message has been received.

The annual Binding ceremony — the communal unbinding and flight — carries the grief of every loss the community has absorbed since the last Binding. When the wings come off and the community flies, the empty space in the sky where a dead member used to be is visible. Everyone sees it. No one fills it. The flight continues around the absence, and the absence is the memorial, and the memorial moves through the air the way the dead moved through the air, and for one night a year the dead are present in the shape of the space they left behind.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Lunarians**

Lunarian children are born into hiding. This shapes everything.

A Lunarian child's first lesson is not language, not walking, not the social skills that other races' children absorb through proximity and play. A Lunarian child's first lesson is stillness. In a mountain settlement where survival depends on not being found, a crying child is a threat. Lunarian infants are not punished for crying — they are held, comforted, and soothed with a specific humming technique that memory-keepers have maintained for centuries, a sub-vocal sound that calms without carrying beyond the room's stone walls. By the time a Lunarian child can walk, they have learned that volume has consequences, and this knowledge is not trauma — it is the architecture of a life lived in hiding, absorbed so early that it becomes the foundation rather than the constraint.

The children fly in secret. During the wing-hour, between dusk and dark, children whose wings have developed enough to carry them (usually by age seven or eight) are allowed short flights within the settlement's perimeter. The flights are supervised, brief, and the most joyful moments in a Lunarian childhood. A Lunarian child in the air for the first time — wings spread, thermal field flickering, the glow of their back painting the darkening sky — is experiencing freedom that the rest of their life will be organized around protecting. They will remember this flight. They will remember every flight. When they bind their wings in the morning and walk through the settlement looking like everyone else, the memory of the air is the thing that makes the binding bearable.

The games are quiet. **Glow-seek** — a hide-and-seek variant played in total darkness where the seekers must find the hiders by detecting the faint thermal glow from their upper backs — teaches the children to control their glow output, dimming it through breath control and emotional regulation. A child who can suppress their glow entirely in the dark has mastered a survival skill that may save their life if the settlement is ever discovered. The game is fun. The skill is lethal serious. The children know both things simultaneously and do not find the coexistence strange, because in a Lunarian childhood, fun and survival have never been separate categories.

What they get in trouble for: carelessness with the memory. Not the formal archive — children are not given fragments until the First Carrying at twenty-five. But every Lunarian child is taught pieces of the community's general history, and a child who repeats community knowledge to an outsider — even innocently, even in a market town where the family has gone to trade — is corrected with an intensity that leaves an impression. The phrase every Lunarian child hears: **"Solmund's wings, your mouth"** — meaning: the knowledge you carry is older than you, more important than you, and your job is to keep it, not to give it away. The phrase is not cruel. It is the weight of three thousand years of persecution compressed into a parental warning, and every Lunarian child who hears it understands, in a way they can't yet articulate, that they are not just a child. They are a vault. The knowledge lives in them the way blood lives in them, and spilling either is the same kind of loss.

The First Carrying at twenty-five is the rite of passage — entrusted with a small piece of community knowledge, to be memorized and recited perfectly after one year. The year is the test. Not the memorization — any Lunarian can memorize. The test is the carrying: living with knowledge in your head that you cannot share, cannot discuss, cannot write down, and must hold perfectly for twelve months while the rest of your life continues around it. A Lunarian who completes the First Carrying has proven that they can be trusted with weight. The weight will only increase. The community knows this. The child who has just become an adult knows this. The knowledge they carry will grow heavier every year until they die, and on the day they die, someone must be ready to take it, and the chain must not break. The chain has not broken in three thousand years. The twenty-five-year-old who just passed their First Carrying is the newest link, and the weight of every link before them rests on the quality of their memory and the discipline of their silence.

### Oni — ~30,000 total worldwide

**Physiology:** Larger than humans, comparable to Orcs or bigger. Distinguished by prominent horns (one to three) from the forehead, skin in saturated colors (red, blue, deep purple, white), raw physical strength exceeding every other race pound-for-pound. An Oni at baseline, zero Dominion, is roughly as strong as a Tempered human. Lifespan: 80-100 years.
**Distribution:** Primarily Caldruun, concentrated in the **Sennaku Highlands** — volcanic plateau where Oni clans have maintained independence for centuries.
**Dominion Tendency:** Devastating Armoring. Compounds with natural strength — an Oni at Forged level cracks ship hulls with bare fists.

**Cultural Role:** Festival-driven and performative. Strength celebrated publicly. Hospitality involves feeding guests until they cannot eat more. Considered rowdy, generous, and deeply dangerous when provoked. The Masqarat has never successfully conscripted Oni in significant numbers — not ideology, just "Oni do not take orders from people they could snap in half."

**Naming:** Short and percussive — one to two syllables for given names. Children named by the mother. An Oni who dislikes their name can take a new one by announcing it publicly. No ceremony. The Masqarat finds this administratively infuriating.

**Food:** Twice the caloric intake of an equivalent human. Four to five meals daily. Highland diet: **highland game** (six hours of slow-roasting), **ashfall grain** (grows in volcanic soil), **storm tea** (bitter, caffeinated — humans describe it as "being punched in the mouth by a plant"). Festival food: whole-roasted game on shield-sized platters and **the Burning Toast** — high-proof spirit literally ignited before consumption. Burns are common and considered festive.

**Art:** **Tattoo and metalwork**. Festival tattoos tell stories — an Oni's body is their autobiography. Tattoo artists are **skin-singers** who narrate the story while inking. Oni metalwork is rougher than Gorundai but more expressive — pieces carry deliberate imperfections considered signatures. A weapon without flaws is soulless.

**Key Holidays:** **The Grand Festival** (7 days, midsummer) — Opening Roar, five days of contests (combat, drinking, storytelling marathons, cooking, skin-singing), one Quiet Day for visits and debts, Closing Feast. Marriages proposed during the Closing Feast are especially auspicious. **The Horn-Night** (when an Oni's horn is broken — permanent) — private gathering, stories told about the horn, fragment incorporated into jewelry or weapon. The horn is not gone — it has been transformed.

**Coming-of-Age:** The **First Bout** at fourteen-sixteen — formal combat, public, festive, violent. Winner and loser both receive first tattoo.

**Death Customs:** Mountaintop pyre. The closest person lights the fire. Attendees drink the Burning Toast and tell stories — must include at least one embarrassing moment. A funeral without laughter is a failure. Storm weather during a funeral: the goddess Kessuura acknowledging the dead. Clear weather: they were unremarkable. The Oni prefer storms.

**Marriage:** The **Challenge Toast** — both partners drink from the same cup of lit spirit. If both finish without spilling, sealed. If one spills, the other decides: accept (marriage starts with a debt) or refuse.

*"If an Oni invites you to drink, clear your schedule. If an Oni invites you to fight, clear your will."*

---


#### Humanization — Oni: The Lived Experience

**A. The Daily Life Paragraph — Oni**

An Oni shepherd in the Sennaku Highlands wakes to thunder, which is to say he wakes to Tuesday. The Sennaku plateau sits between four and seven thousand feet above sea level, volcanic heat rising from below, storm systems rolling in from every direction, and the sound of lightning striking the same peak for the third consecutive hour — **kamiuchi**, god-drumming, which the Oni consider neither dangerous nor notable. It is weather. You dress for weather. He dresses: leather trousers, heavy boots soled for volcanic rock, a coat made from storm-leather (animal hide treated with volcanic mineral oil until it repels water and weighs twice what it should), and nothing on the torso because the geothermal heat rising through the rock makes the highlands warm even in the rain, and an Oni who covers their chest in the highlands is either sick or a tourist.

Breakfast is enormous. Two pounds of highland game — leftover from last night's roast, eaten cold, pulled from the bone with hands and tusks — a mound of ashfall grain porridge that makes Gorundai gorrash look like a delicacy, and a cup of storm tea that he drinks the way Oni drink everything: all at once, in one long swallow, because the tea is bitter enough that sipping it would require acknowledging the taste. He eats with his family — his partner, their three children, his partner's mother who moved in four years ago and shows no sign of leaving and who has opinions about his cooking that she shares at volume and that he absorbs with the specific Oni patience reserved for elders who have earned the right to be impossible.

The walk to the grazing terraces takes forty minutes through terrain that would kill a less-footed person. Volcanic rock, rain-slicked, with thermal vents that produce jets of steam across the path at unpredictable intervals. He knows the vent pattern the way a Morventhi knows their corridor — by feel, by the heat on the soles of his boots, by the faint sulfur-shift in the air that precedes a steam burst by four seconds. The herd is ashfall goats — tough, stupid animals bred for volcanic conditions, whose meat is the highlands' dietary foundation and whose stubbornness is, according to every Oni shepherd who has ever lived, a personal insult from Bhoraam.

He tends the herd until midday. The work is physical — carrying goats out of crevasses they've wandered into, repairing stone fences that the last tremor displaced, checking the geothermal water channels that feed the grazing terraces. At noon he eats again — more meat, more grain, storm tea from a flask that keeps it hot through volcanic ambient heat. Afternoon is the same. The work is repetitive and the repetition is not boredom — it is rhythm, the slow pulse of a life measured by the herd's needs and the mountain's moods. He talks to the goats. The goats do not respond. He considers this evidence of their inferior character.

Evening is the gathering. Oni settlements eat together — not always, not every night, but often enough that eating alone carries a slight charge of concern. The table is loud. The food is more — another full meal, the fourth of the day, because an Oni's metabolism burns through calories the way the mountain burns through stone. His children fight over the last piece of roasted game. His partner's mother tells a story about the Grand Festival of thirty years ago that he has heard forty times and that gets louder with each telling. He does not interrupt. An Oni who interrupts an elder's story at the table is inviting a response that will be remembered and retold at the next festival, and the last thing he wants is to become the cautionary tale his partner's mother has been trying to turn him into for four years.

He falls asleep to the mountain. Not the thunder — the mountain itself. The deep, subsonic vibration of volcanic activity far below the surface, a sound that isn't heard so much as felt, in the bones, in the teeth, in the stone platform beneath the sleeping mat. An Oni who leaves the highlands reports that the world feels hollow without it — as if the ground has stopped speaking and the silence is wrong. They come back. They always come back. The mountain holds its people the way a hand holds a stone: not gently, but completely.

---

**B. The Contradiction Paragraph — Oni**

The Oni say they will never be governed. This is the foundational principle — the reason their Wardensea enlistment rate is 0.3%, the reason the Masqarat has never successfully conscripted them, the reason Kuldhara Mabaan holds the Sennaku Highlands as personal territory and the central authority has decided that the cost of challenging her exceeds the benefit. The Oni distrust centralized power because centralized power produced Rokkhan Setsuda, the Second Age Oni warlord who orchestrated atrocity on a continental scale. "Never again" is not a political position for the Oni. It is ancestral memory. Power concentrated in one person destroyed the world. The Oni will not allow it. They say this at every Grand Festival, in every skin-singing narrative, in every story told to every child.

And yet the Oni follow Kuldhara. A single Conqueror who holds territory by personal force, who commands a Warband that operates outside any institutional check, whose word in the Sennaku Highlands is law in every way that matters. The Oni did not elect Kuldhara. They did not consent to her authority through any process. She arrived, she was stronger than everyone, and the highlands organized around her presence the way iron filings organize around a magnet. The Oni who say they will never be governed have, functionally, submitted to personal rule by a woman who commands storms.

The Oni resolution of this contradiction is elegant and possibly dishonest: Kuldhara is not a ruler. She is a force of nature. The Oni do not follow her the way subjects follow a king — they orient to her the way sailors orient to the weather. She does not issue laws. She issues challenges. She does not command obedience. She earns it through continuous demonstration that she is the strongest thing in any room. The Oni framework allows for this because Oni culture has always organized around strength — the strongest leads the hunt, the strongest speaks first at the fire, the strongest pours the first toast. Kuldhara is simply the strongest at the largest scale. The Oni call this "natural hierarchy" and distinguish it from "imposed hierarchy" (Setsuda, the Masqarat, any authority that claims the right to rule without demonstrating the strength to back it). The distinction is important to the Oni. Whether it is real is a question they do not ask, because asking it would require admitting that their rejection of governance and their acceptance of Kuldhara are, structurally, the same thing.

---

**C. The Prejudice and Friction Paragraph — Oni**

What Oni say about other races at the festival fire, after the second Burning Toast, when the volume has reached the level that other races consider shouting and the Oni consider conversational:

Humans are small and they think too much. An Oni who encounters a human deliberating over a decision will wait approximately thirty seconds before offering to make the decision for them, which the human will interpret as aggression and the Oni will interpret as generosity. The Oni do not dislike humans. They find humans puzzling — all that energy, all that ambition, and they spend half of it in meetings. "Humans have meetings about meetings. We have meetings that are also fights. Ours end faster." The exception is Bastion Sea humans, who drink hard, work hard, and fight when they need to. The Oni consider these the best humans. Everyone else is a work in progress.

Gorundai are blood. The two races share Caldruun, share a respect for physical strength, and share a cultural understanding that the direct path between two points is the correct one. An Oni and a Gorundai in a tavern together will drink, argue, and possibly fight, and all three activities are expressions of the same bond. The friction is about discipline: Gorundai are organized. Oni are not. A Gorundai who watches an Oni approach a problem through improvisation and raw force feels the way a Varrek feels watching a Gorundai forge by feel — horrified and reluctantly impressed. The Gorundai think the Oni are reckless. The Oni think the Gorundai forgot how to have fun. Both are right. Both will drink together anyway.

Thalessi are aliens. The Oni cannot comprehend a people who take six months to decide whether to kiss someone. The cultural gap between Oni directness and Thalessi deliberation is the widest in the world, and cross-cultural interactions between the two races produce a mutual bewilderment so thorough it circles back around to fascination. An Oni who visits Vethara spends the entire time vibrating with the effort of not being the loudest thing in the room. A Thalessi who visits the Grand Festival spends the entire time vibrating with the effort of not being the quietest. Both find the experience unforgettable and never want to repeat it. Some do anyway. The resulting friendships are, against all probability, among the world's most durable.

Morventhi make sense. Underground builders who bathe communally, speak directly, and treat pleasure as a social good — the Oni find the Morventhi comprehensible in a way they find few other races. The warmrooms remind them of the hot springs. The echo-singing reminds them of the skin-singers. The honesty principle reminds them of themselves. An Oni who visits the Undersprawl and an Oni who visits Vethara will have very different stories to tell, and the Undersprawl story will be told with warmth.

Khari are friends. The two cultures share a festival energy, a love of gathering, and an approach to life that prioritizes experience over accumulation. Khari warrens that pass through the highlands during Grand Festival season are absorbed into the celebration with a completeness that blurs the distinction between guest and host. The Oni feed the Khari until they can't move. The Khari teach the Oni songs that the Oni butcher enthusiastically. Both sides consider this an excellent arrangement.

Rathai are small, fast, and they fix things. The Oni respect the Rathai with the specific warmth reserved for people who are useful and unpretentious. An Oni whose Grimoire breaks will find a Rathai who can fix it, pay them fairly, and marvel at how someone so small can work so quickly. The size differential is a running source of Oni humor that the Rathai tolerate because the Oni tell the jokes to their faces and the Rathai prefer honest mockery to polite invisibility.

Varrek are confusing. A people who measure everything and express nothing. The Oni find this baffling and slightly sad. "They know the exact temperature of the fire and they've forgotten to feel warm."

Lunarians are sacred. The Oni remember storms. Kessuura is their patron god, the storms are Kessuura's language, and the Silverborn were Kessuura's closest allies in the First Age. The Lunarians carry the Silverborn's light. An Oni who meets a Lunarian will offer storm tea — the highest Oni hospitality — and sit in uncharacteristic silence, listening, because when the descendants of the gods' allies speak, even the Oni know when to stop talking.

What other races say about the Oni: "They're loud." First. Always first. Then: "They're loyal." Then, quietly: "They're bigger than us." The Oni hear all three and respond to the first with pride, the second with a nod, and the third with a grin that shows every tusk. The Oni know what they are. They have never pretended to be anything else. This is either their greatest strength or their greatest limitation, and the Oni consider the question irrelevant.

---

**D. The Love and Grief Paragraph — Oni**

An Oni in love is an Oni who picks fights. Not with the person they love — with everyone else. The thrakkenvoss that the Gorundai do quietly, the Oni do at full volume: challenging people to contests, drinking competitions, feats of strength, anything that lets them demonstrate value in the general vicinity of the person who has not yet noticed that every demonstration is aimed at them. An Oni who suddenly becomes reckless is an Oni who is trying to impress someone. Their friends know. The target eventually knows. The mountain knows because the mountain has heard this story ten thousand times and the thunder sounds the same every time.

The approach is the Challenge Toast in miniature: a dare. An Oni interested in someone challenges them to a drink, a fight, a climb, a contest. The challenge is the confession. Accepting is accepting the interest. Winning earns the right to initiate. Losing earns respect, which in Oni culture is not a consolation prize — respect is the currency that all other currency is measured against, and an Oni who loses a courtship challenge gracefully has demonstrated more about their character than an Oni who wins without breaking a sweat.

Oni love once established is physical, celebratory, and sustained at a volume that would exhaust any other race. An Oni partner does not whisper endearments. They declare them across a room, in public, with the full force of lungs designed for highland altitudes where the air is thin and the voice must carry. An Oni couple arguing at the table is not a crisis — it is communication at operational volume. An Oni couple being tender at the table is the same volume but softer in content, and the shift between the two is so subtle that outsiders often can't tell the difference while every Oni in earshot reads it perfectly.

The deep thing about Oni love is the tattoo. An Oni's body is their autobiography — skin-singers narrate the story while inking, and every major life event is written on the body. When an Oni takes a partner, the partner's mark enters the narrative. Not a name — a moment. The first challenge. The first night. The specific image that the skin-singer chooses to represent what the partnership means. An Oni's body tells you who they've loved because the love is in the ink, and the ink does not fade, and even if the partnership ends, the mark remains. An Oni carries their loves on their skin the way a Gorundai carries their dead in iron — permanently, visibly, without the option of forgetting.

Oni grief is fire.

The pyre is lit by the closest person. The mountaintop is chosen for the view — the Oni believe the dead should see the sky. The attendees drink the Burning Toast — high-proof spirit, literally ignited, consumed while the pyre burns. The stories are mandatory. They must include at least one embarrassing moment. A funeral without laughter is a failure. This is not denial of grief — it is the Oni insistence that the dead were alive, fully alive, ridiculously alive, and the proof of that life is the absurdity they generated. An Oni funeral that is only sad has missed the point. The dead person would be offended.

Storm weather during a funeral means Kessuura acknowledges the dead. This is not metaphor to the Oni — it is literal. The goddess of storms, who has raged since Tavrendis was sealed, recognizes a soul departing and sends weather. Clear skies mean the dead were unremarkable. The Oni prefer storms. An Oni funeral held under clear sky carries a faint, unspoken sting that the family will never mention and the community will note quietly: the goddess was not moved. This is probably superstition. The Oni do not care about probably.

After the pyre: the Horn-Night's logic applies. Something has been lost. Something is transformed. The Oni who loved the dead carry forward in the way the Oni carry everything — loudly, physically, with the specific Oni conviction that sitting quietly with grief is an insult to the life that generated it. A grieving Oni works harder, fights harder, drinks harder, and tells the dead person's stories louder, and this escalation is not avoidance — it is the Oni grief ritual, performed daily, without ceremony, until the volume adjusts and the stories settle into the permanent repertoire and the dead become part of the noise the living make. The Oni do not have a word for closure. They have a word for continuation, and it sounds like thunder.

---

**E. The Children Paragraph — Oni**

Oni children are large at birth, louder by noon, and climbing something by sunset. An Oni infant weighs eight to twelve pounds — Oni mothers are celebrated for producing them and supported by the entire clan, with the best food, a permanent seat at the fire, and absolute authority over the nearest cooking pot. A pregnant Oni who requests something receives it. This is not courtesy. It is survival instinct dressed as tradition: an Oni woman in her third trimester is hormonally primed for a level of assertiveness that even other Oni find impressive, and the community learned long ago that compliance is simpler than argument.

By three, an Oni child has attempted to fight everything reachable. By five, they have attempted to fight things that are not reachable by climbing to reach them. Oni parenting is the art of managing a creature that is, pound for pound, the strongest child of any race and has the impulse control of a weather system. The Oni approach: let them fight, let them fall, let them scream, and feed them immediately after because an Oni child who is crying and hungry will solve for the hunger first and forget the crying.

The games are violent. Not cruel — but physical at a level that would close a human school. **Horn-tag** — a chase game where the "it" player attempts to touch opponents with their horns — is the Oni equivalent of catch, played from age four, and resulting in bruises, horn-scratches, and a social hierarchy based on who can dodge the fastest. The first time an Oni child draws blood in horn-tag (accidental or otherwise) is an event noted with alarm by non-Oni observers and with casual interest by Oni parents, who check the wound, confirm it's shallow, and send both children back to the game. "Horns grow. Skin heals. Cowardice doesn't."

What they get in trouble for: not being loud enough. This sounds absurd to other races and is deeply serious within Oni culture. An Oni child who is quiet — who withdraws, who stops wrestling, who sits alone — is a child who is communicating something wrong, and the community responds with the specific Oni urgency reserved for emergencies. The phrase every Oni child hears is not a demand for quiet or discipline. It is: **"Kess-varra duun!"** — roughly, "voice up!" Meaning: if something is wrong, say it. If you're hurt, shout. If you're scared, roar. If you're happy, be happier.
